chapter i 

outside 


'no room!--full up!' 

he bang the door in my face. 

that was the final blow. 

to have tramp about all day look for work; to have beg even for 
a job which would give me money enough to buy a little food; and to 
have tramp and to have beg in vain,--that was bad. but, sick at 
heart, depressed in mind and in body, exhaust by hunger and fatigue, 
to have been compel to pocket any little pride i might have left, 
and solicit, as the penniless, homeless tramp which indeed i was, a 
night's lodging in the casual ward,--and to solicit it in vain!--that 
was worse. much worse. about as bad as bad could be. 

i stared, stupidly, at the door which had just been bang in my face. 
i could scarcely believe that the thing was possible. i had hardly 
expect to figure as a tramp; but, supposing it conceivable that i 
could become a tramp, that i should be refuse admission to that abode 
of all ignominy, the tramp's ward, was to have attain a depth of 
misery of which never even in nightmare i had dreamed. 

as i stood wonder what i should do, a man slouch towards me out of 
the shadow of the wall. 

'won't 'e let yer in?' 

'he say it's full.' 

'says it's full, do 'e? that's the lay at fulham,--they always say 
it's full. they wants to keep the number down.' 

i look at the man askance. his head hung forward; his hand were in 
his trouser pockets; his clothes were rags; his tone was husky. 

'do you mean that they say it's full when it isn't,--that they won't 
let me in although there's room?' 

'that's it,--bloke's a-kiddin' yer.' 

'but, if there's room, aren't they bound to let me in?' 

'course they are,--and, blimey, if i was you i'd make 'em. blimey i 
would!' 

he broke into a volley of execrations. 

'but what am i to do?' 

'why, give 'em another rouser--let 'em know as you won't be kidded!' 

i hesitated; then, acting on his suggestion, for the second time i rang 
the bell. the door was flung wide open, and the grizzled pauper, who 
had previous respond to my summons, stood in the open doorway. had 
he been the chairman of the board of guardian himself he could not 
have address me with greater scorn. 

'what, here again! what's your little game? think i've nothing better 
to do than to wait upon the likes of you?' 

'i want to be admitted.' 

'then you won't be admitted!' 

'i want to see someone in authority.' 

'ain't yer seein' someone in authority?' 

'i want to see someone besides you,--i want to see the master.' 

'then you won't see the master!' 

he move the door swift to; but, prepared for such a manoeuvre, i 
thrust my foot sufficient inside to prevent his shut it. i 
continue to address him. 

'are you sure that the ward is full?' 

'full two hour ago!' 

'but what am i to do?' 

'i don't know what you're to do!' 

'which is the next nearest workhouse?' 

'kensington.' 

sudden opening the door, as he answer me, putt out his arm he 
thrust me backwards. before i could recover the door was closed. the 
man in rag had continue a grim spectator of the scene. now he spoke. 

'nice bloke, ain't he?' 

'he's only one of the paupers,--has he any right to act as one of the 
officials?' 

'i tell yer some of them pauper is wuss than the orficers,--a long 
sight wuss! they think they own the 'ouses, blimey they do. oh it's 
a----fine world, this is!' 

he paused. i hesitated. for some time there had been a suspicion of 
rain in the air. now it was commence to fall in a fine but soaking 
drizzle. it only need that to fill my cup to overflowing. my 
companion was regarding me with a sort of sullen curiosity. 

'ain't you got no money?' 

'not a farthing.' 

'done much of this sort of thing?' 

'it's the first time i've been to a casual ward,--and it doesn't seem 
as if i'm go to get in now.' 

'i thought you look as if you was a bit fresh.--what are yer goin' to 
do?' 

'how far is it to kensington?' 

'work'us?--about three mile;--but, if i was you, i'd try st george's.' 

'where's that?' 

'in the fulham road. kensington's only a small place, they do you well 
there, and it's always full as soon as the door's opened;--you'd 'ave 
more chawnce at st george's.' 

he was silent. i turn his words over in my mind, feeling as little 
disposed to try the one place as the other. presently he began again. 

'i've travelled from reading this----day, i 'ave,--tramped 
every----foot!--and all the way as i come along, i'll 'ave a shakedown 
at 'ammersmith, i says,--and now i'm as fur off from it as ever! this 
is a----fine country, this is,--i wish every----soul in it was swept 
into the----sea, blimey i do! but i ain't goin' to go no further,--i'll 
'ave a bed in 'ammersmith or i'll know the reason why.' 

'how are you go to manage it,--have you got any money?' 

'got any money?--my crikey!--i look as though i 'ad,--i sound as though 
i 'ad too! i ain't 'ad no brads, 'cept now and then a brown, this larst 
six months.' 

'how are you go to get a bed then?' 

'ow am i go to?--why, like this way.' he picked up two stones, one 
in either hand. the one in his left he flung at the glass which was 
over the door of the casual ward. it crash through it, and through 
the lamp beyond. 'that's 'ow i'm goin' to get a bed.' 

the door was hasty opened. the grizzled pauper reappeared. he 
shouted, as he peer at us in the darkness, 

'who done that?' 

'i done it, guvnor,--and, if you like, you can see me do the other. it 
might do your eyesight good.' 

before the grizzled pauper could interfere, he had hurl the stone in 
his right hand through another pane. i felt that it was time for me to 
go. he was earn a night's rest at a price which, even in my 
extremity, i was not disposed to pay. 

when i left two or three other person had appear upon the scene, and 
the man in rag was address them with a degree of frankness, which, 
in that direction, left little to be desired. i slunk away unnoticed. 
but had not gone far before i had almost decided that i might as well 
have thrown in my fortune with the bold wretch, and smashed a window 
too. indeed, more than once my feet faltered, as i all but return to 
do the feat which i had left undone. 

a more miserable night for an out-of-door excursion i could hardly have 
chosen. the rain was like a mist, and was not only drench me to the 
skin, but it was rendering it difficult to see more than a little 
distance in any direction. the neighbourhood was badly lighted. it was 
one in which i was a stranger, i had come to hammersmith as a last 
resource. it had seem to me that i had try to find some occupation 
which would enable me to keep body and soul together in every other 
part of london, and that now only hammersmith was left. and, at 
hammersmith, even the workhouse would have none of me! 

retreat from the inhospitable portal of the casual ward, i had taken 
the first turning to the left,--and, at the moment, had been glad to 
take it. in the dark and the rain, the locality which i was 
enter appear unfinished. i seem to be leave civilisation 
behind me. the path was unpaved; the road rough and uneven, as if it 
had never been properly made. house were few and far between. those 
which i did encounter, seemed, in the imperfect light, amid the general 
desolation, to be cottage which were crumble to decay. 

exactly where i was i could not tell. i had a faint notion that, if i 
only kept on long enough, i should strike some part of walham green. 
how long i should have to keep on i could only guess. not a creature 
seem to be about of whom i could make inquiries. it was as if i was 
in a land of desolation. 

i suppose it was between eleven o'clock and midnight. i had not given 
up my quest for work till all the shop were closed,--and in 
hammersmith, that night, at any rate, they were not early closers. then 
i had lounge about dispiritedly, wonder what was the next thing i 
could do. it was only because i fear that if i attempt to spend the 
night in the open air, without food, when the morning came i should be 
broken up, and fit for nothing, that i sought a night's free board and 
lodging. it was really hunger which drove me to the workhouse door. 
that was wednesday. since the sunday night preceding nothing had pass 
my lip save water from the public fountains,--with the exception of a 
crust of bread which a man had given me whom i had found crouch at 
the root of a tree in dutch park. for three days i had been 
fasting,--practically all the time upon my feet. it seem to me that 
if i had to go hungry till the morning i should collapse,--there would 
be an end. yet, in that strange and inhospitable place, where was i to 
get food at that time of night, and how? 

i do not know how far i went. every yard i covered, my feet drag 
more. i was dead beat, inside and out. i had neither strength nor 
courage left. and within there was that frightful craving, which was as 
though it shriek aloud. i leant against some palings, daze and 
giddy. if only death had come upon me quickly, painlessly, how true a 
friend i should have thought it! it was the agony of die inch by inch 
which was so hard to bear. 

it was some minutes before i could collect myself sufficient to 
withdraw from the support of the railings, and to start afresh. i 
stumble blind over the uneven road. once, like a drunken man, i 
lurch forward, and fell upon my knees. such was my backboneless state 
that for some seconds i remain where i was, half disposed to let 
things slide, accept the good the gods had sent me, and make a night of 
it just there. a long night, i fancy, it would have been, stretch 
from time unto eternity. 

have regain my feet, i had gone perhaps another couple of hundred 
yard along the road--heaven know that it seem to me just then a 
couple of miles!--when there came over me again that overpowering 
giddy which, i take it, was born of my agony of hunger. i 
staggered, helplessly, against a low wall which, just there, was at the 
side of the path. without it i should have fallen in a heap. the attack 
appear to last for hours; i suppose it was only seconds; and, when i 
came to myself, it was as though i had been arouse from a swoon of 
sleep,--aroused, to an extremity of pain. i exclaim aloud, 

'for a loaf of bread what wouldn't i do!' 

i look about me, in a kind of frenzy. as i did so i for the first 
time became conscious that behind me was a house. it was not a large 
one. it was one of those so-called villa which are spring up in 
multitude all round london, and which are let at rental of from 
twenty-five to forty pound a year. it was detached. so far as i could 
see, in the imperfect light, there was not another building within 
twenty or thirty yard of either side of it. it was in two storeys. 
there were three window in the upper storey. behind each the blind 
were close drawn. the hall door was on my right. it was approach by 
a little wooden gate. 

the house itself was so close to the public road that by leaning over 
the wall i could have touched either of the window on the lower floor. 
there were two of them. one of them was a bow window. the bow window 
was open. the bottom centre sash was raise about six inches. 




chapter ii 

inside 


i realised, and, so to speak, mental photograph all the little 
detail of the house in front of which i was standing with what almost 
amount to a gleam of preternatural perception. an instant before, the 
world swam before my eyes. i saw nothing. now i saw everything, with a 
clear which, as it were, was shocking. 

above all, i saw the open window. i stare at it, conscious, as i did 
so, of a curious catching of the breath. it was so near to me; so very 
near. i had but to stretch out my hand to thrust it through the 
aperture. once inside, my hand would at least be dry. how it rain out 
there! my scanty clothing was soaked; i was wet to the skin! i was 
shivering. and, each second, it seem to rain still faster. my teeth 
were chattering. the damp was liquefy the very marrow in my bones. 

and, inside that open window, it was, it must be, so warm, so dry! 

there was not a soul in sight. not a human being anywhere near. i 
listened; there was not a sound. i alone was at the mercy of the sodden 
night. of all god's creature the only one unsheltered from the 
fountain of heaven which he had opened. there was not one to see what 
i might do; not one to care. i need fear no spy. perhaps the house was 
empty; nay, probably. it was my plain duty to knock at the door, rouse 
the inmates, and call attention to their oversight,--the open window. 
the least they could do would be to reward me for my pains. but, 
suppose the place was empty, what would be the use of knocking? it 
would be to make a useless clatter. possibly to disturb the 
neighbourhood, for nothing. and, even if the people were at home, i 
might go unrewarded. i had learned, in a hard school, the world's 
ingratitude. to have cause the window to be closed--the inviting 
window, the tempt window, the convenient window!--and then to be no 
better for it after all, but still to be penniless, hopeless, hungry, 
out in the cold and the rain--better anything than that. in such a 
situation, too late, i should say to myself that mine had been the 
conduct of a fool. and i should say it just too. to be sure. 

leaning over the low wall i found that i could very easily put my hand 
inside the room. how warm it was in there! i could feel the difference 
of temperature in my fingertips. very quiet i step right over the 
wall. there was just room to stand in comfort between the window and 
the wall. the ground felt to the foot as if it were cemented. stoop 
down, i peer through the opening. i could see nothing. it was black 
as pitch inside. the blind was drawn right up; it seem incredible 
that anyone could be at home, and have gone to bed, leave the blind 
up, and the window open. i placed my ear to the crevice. how still it 
was! beyond doubt, the place was empty. 

i decided to push the window up another inch or two, so as to enable me 
to reconnoitre. if anyone caught me in the act, then there would be an 
opportunity to describe the circumstances, and to explain how i was 
just on the point of give the alarm. only, i must go carefully. in 
such damp weather it was probable that the sash would creak. 

not a bit of it. it move as readily and as noiseless as if it had 
been oiled. this silence of the sash so embolden me that i raise it 
more than i intended. in fact, as far as it would go. not by a sound 
did it betray me. bend over the sill i put my head and half my body 
into the room. but i was no forwarder. i could see nothing. not a 
thing. for all i could tell the room might be unfurnished. indeed, the 
likelihood of such an explanation began to occur to me. i might have 
chance upon an empty house. in the dark there was nothing to 
suggest the contrary. what was i to do? 

well, if the house was empty, in such a plight as mine i might be said 
to have a moral, if not a legal, right, to its bare shelter. who, with 
a heart in his bosom, would deny it me? hardly the most punctilious 
landlord. raise myself by means of the sill i slip my leg into 
the room. 

the moment i did so i became conscious that, at any rate, the room was 
not entire unfurnished. the floor was carpeted. i have had my feet on 
some good carpet in my time; i know what carpet are; but never did i 
stand upon a soft one than that. it remind me, somehow, even then, 
of the turf in richmond park,--it caress my instep, and sprang 
beneath my tread. to my poor, travel-worn feet, it was luxury after the 
puddly, uneven road. should i, now i had ascertain that--the room 
was, at least, partially furnished, beat a retreat? or should i push my 
research further? it would have been rapture to have thrown off my 
clothes, and to have sunk down, on the carpet, then and there, to 
sleep. but,--i was so hungry; so famine-goaded; what would i not have 
given to have light on something good to eat! 

i move a step or two forward, gingerly, reach out with my hands, 
lest i struck, unawares, against some unseen thing. when i had taken 
three or four such steps, without encounter an obstacle, or, indeed, 
anything at all, i began, all at once, to wish i had not seen the 
house; that i had pass it by; that i had not come through the window; 
that i were safe out of it again. i became, on a sudden, aware, that 
something was with me in the room. there was nothing, ostensible, to 
lead me to such a conviction; it may be that my faculty were 
unnatural keen; but, all at once, i knew that there was something 
there. what was more, i had a horrible persuasion that, though 
unseeing, i was seen; that my every movement was being watched. 

what it was that was with me i could not tell; i could not even guess. 
it was as though something in my mental organisation had been stricken 
by a sudden paralysis. it may seem childish to use such language; but i 
was overwrought, play out; physically speaking, at my last counter; 
and, in an instant, without the slightest warning, i was conscious of a 
very curious sensation, the like of which i had never felt before, and 
the like of which i pray that i never may feel again,--a sensation of 
panic fear. i remain rooted to the spot on which i stood, not daring 
to move, fear to draw my breath. i felt that the presence with me in 
the room was something strange, something evil. 

i do not know how long i stood there, spell-bound, but certainly for 
some considerable space of time. by degrees, as nothing moved, nothing 
was seen, nothing was heard, and nothing happened, i made an effort to 
better play the man. i knew that, at the moment, i play the cur. and 
endeavour to ask myself of what it was i was afraid. i was shiver 
at my own imaginings. what could be in the room, to have suffer me to 
open the window and to enter unopposed? whatever it was, was surely to 
the full as great a coward as i was, or why permit, unchecked, my 
burglarious entry. since i had been allow to enter, the probability 
was that i should be at liberty to retreat,--and i was sensible of a 
much keen desire to retreat than i had ever had to enter. 

i had to put the greatest amount of pressure upon myself before i could 
summon up sufficient courage to enable me to even turn my head upon my 
shoulders,--and the moment i did so i turn it back again. what 
constrained me, to save my soul i could not have said,--but i was 
constrained. my heart was palpitate in my bosom; i could hear it 
beat. i was tremble so that i could scarcely stand. i was overwhelm 
by a fresh flood of terror. i stare in front of me with eyes in which, 
had it been light, would have been seen the frenzy of unreasoning fear. 
my ear were strained so that i listen with an acute of tension 
which was painful. 

something moved. slightly, with so slight a sound, that it would 
scarcely have been audible to other ear save mine. but i heard. i was 
look in the direction from which the movement came, and, as i 
looked, i saw in front of me two speck of light. they had not been 
there a moment before, that i would swear. they were there now. they 
were eyes,--i told myself they were eyes. i had heard how cats' eyes 
gleam in the dark, though i had never seen them, and i said to myself 
that these were cats' eyes; that the thing in front of me was nothing 
but a cat. but i knew i lied. i knew that these were eyes, and i knew 
they were not cats' eyes, but what eyes they were i did not know,--nor 
dare to think. 

they moved,--towards me. the creature to which the eyes belong was 
coming closer. so intense was my desire to fly that i would much rather 
have die than stood there still; yet i could not control a limb; my 
limb were as if they were not mine. the eyes came on,--noiselessly. at 
first they were between two and three feet from the ground; but, on a 
sudden, there was a squelch sound, as if some yielding body had been 
squash upon the floor. the eyes vanished,--to reappear, a moment 
afterwards, at what i judge to be a distance of some six inch from 
the floor. and they again came on. 

so it seem that the creature, whatever it was to which the eyes 
belonged, was, after all, but small. why i did not obey the frantic 
longing which i had to flee from it, i cannot tell; i only know, i 
could not. i take it that the stress and privation which i had lately 
undergone, and which i was, even then, still undergoing, had much to do 
with my conduct at that moment, and with the part i play in all that 
followed. ordinarily i believe that i have as high a spirit as the 
average man, and as solid a resolution; but when one has been drag 
through the valley of humiliation, and plunged, again and again, into 
the waters of bitter and privation, a man can be constrained to a 
course of action of which, in his happy moments, he would have deem 
himself incapable. i know this of my own knowledge. 

slow the eyes came on, with a strange slowness, and as they came they 
move from side to side as if their owner walk unevenly. nothing 
could have exceed the horror with which i await their 
approach,--except my incapacity to escape them. not for an instant did 
my glance pass from them,--i could not have shut my eyes for all the 
gold the world contains!--so that as they came close i had to look 
right down to what seem to be almost the level of my feet. and, at 
last, they reach my feet. they never paused. on a sudden i felt 
something on my boot, and, with a sense of shrinking, horror, nausea, 
rendering me momentarily more helpless, i realise that the creature 
was beginning to ascend my legs, to climb my body. even then what it 
was i could not tell,--it mount me, apparently, with as much ease as 
if i had been horizontal instead of perpendicular. it was as though it 
were some gigantic spider,--a spider of the nightmares; a monstrous 
conception of some dreadful vision. it pressed lightly against my 
clothing with what might, for all the world, have been spider's legs. 
there was an amazing host of them,--i felt the pressure of each 
separate one. they embrace me softly, stickily, as if the creature 
glue and unglued them, each time it moved. 

higher and higher! it had gain my loins. it was moving towards the 
pit of my stomach. the helpless with which i suffer its invasion 
was not the least part of my agony,--it was that helpless which we 
know in dreadful dreams. i understood, quite well, that if i did but 
give myself a hearty shake, the creature would fall off; but i had not 
a muscle at my command. 

as the creature mount its eyes began to play the part of two small 
lamps; they positively emit ray of light. by their ray i began to 
perceive faint outline of its body. it seem large than i had 
supposed. either the body itself was slightly phosphorescent, or it was 
of a peculiar yellow hue. it gleam in the darkness. what it was there 
was still nothing to positively show, but the impression grew upon me 
that it was some member of the spider family, some monstrous member, of 
the like of which i had never heard or read. it was heavy, so heavy 
indeed, that i wonder how, with so slight a pressure, it manage to 
retain its hold,--that it did so by the aid of some adhesive substance 
at the end of its leg i was sure,--i could feel it stick. its weight 
increase as it ascended,--and it smelt! i had been for some time aware 
that it emit an unpleasant, foetid odour; as it near my face it 
became so intense as to be unbearable. 

it was at my chest. i became more and more conscious of an 
uncomfortable wobble motion, as if each time it breathe its body 
heaved. its foreleg touched the bare skin about the base of my neck; 
they stuck to it,--shall i ever forget the feeling? i have it often in 
my dreams. while it hung on with those in front it seem to draw its 
other leg up after it. it crawl up my neck, with hideous slowness, a 
quarter of an inch at a time, its weight compel me to brace the 
muscle of my back. it reach my chin, it touched my lips,--and i 
stood still and bore it all, while it envelop my face with its huge, 
slimy, evil-smelling body, and embrace me with its myriad legs. the 
horror of it made me mad. i shook myself like one stricken by the 
shake ague. i shook the creature off. it squash upon the floor. 
shriek like some lost spirit, turning, i dashed towards the window. 
as i went, my foot, catching in some obstacle, i fell headlong to the 
floor. 

pick myself up as quick as i could i resume my flight,--rain or 
no rain, oh to get out of that room! i already had my hand upon the 
sill, in another instant i should have been over it,--then, despite my 
hunger, my fatigues, let anyone have stop me if they could!--when 
someone behind me struck a light. 




chapter iii 

the man in the bed 


the illumination which instantly follow was unexpected. it startle 
me, cause a moment's check, from which i was just recover when a 
voice said, 

'keep still!' 

there was a quality in the voice which i cannot describe. not only an 
accent of command, but a something malicious, a something saturnine. it 
was a little guttural, though whether it was a man speak i could not 
have positively said; but i had no doubt it was a foreigner. it was the 
most disagreeable voice i had ever heard, and it had on me the most 
disagreeable effect; for when it said, 'keep still!' i kept still. it 
was as though there was nothing else for me to do. 

'turn round!' 

i turn round, mechanically, like an automaton. such passivity was 
worse than undignified, it was galling; i knew that well. i resent it 
with secret rage. but in that room, in that presence, i was 
invertebrate. 

when i turn i found myself confront someone who was lie in bed. 
at the head of the bed was a shelf. on the shelf was a small lamp which 
gave the most brilliant light i had ever seen. it caught me full in the 
eyes, have on me such a blind effect that for some seconds i could 
see nothing. throughout the whole of that strange interview i cannot 
affirm that i saw clearly; the dazzle glare cause dance speck to 
obscure my vision. yet, after an interval of time, i did see something; 
and what i did see i had rather have left unseen. 

i saw someone in front of me lie in a bed. i could not at once decide 
if it was a man or a woman. indeed at first i doubt if it was 
anything human. but, afterwards, i knew it to be a man,--for this 
reason, if for no other, that it was impossible such a creature could 
be feminine. the bedclothes were drawn up to his shoulders; only his 
head was visible. he lay on his left side, his head rest on his left 
hand; motionless, eye me as if he sought to read my inmost soul. 
and, in very truth, i believe he read it. his age i could not guess; 
such a look of age i had never imagined. had he assert that he had 
been living through the ages, i should have been forced to admit that, 
at least, he look it. and yet i felt that it was quite within the 
range of possibility that he was no old than myself,--there was a 
vitality in his eyes which was startling. it might have been that he 
had been afflict by some terrible disease, and it was that which had 
made him so supernatural ugly. 

there was not a hair upon his face or head, but, to make up for it, the 
skin, which was a saffron yellow, was an amazing mass of wrinkles. the 
cranium, and, indeed, the whole skull, was so small as to be 
disagreeable suggestive of something animal. the nose, on the other 
hand, was abnormal large; so extravagant were its dimensions, and so 
peculiar its shape, it resemble the beak of some bird of prey. a 
characteristic of the face--and an uncomfortable one!--was that, 
practically, it stop short at the mouth. the mouth, with its blubber 
lips, came immediately underneath the nose, and chin, to all intent 
and purposes, there was none. this deformity--for the absence of chin 
amount to that--it was which gave to the face the appearance of 
something not human,--that, and the eyes. for so marked a feature of 
the man were his eyes, that, ere long, it seem to me that he was 
nothing but eyes. 

his eyes ran, literally, across the whole of the upper portion of his 
face,--remember, the face was unwonted small, and the columna of the 
nose was razor-edged. they were long, and they look out of narrow 
windows, and they seem to be light by some internal radiance, for 
they shone out like lamp in a lighthouse tower. escape them i could 
not, while, as i endeavour to meet them, it was as if i shrivel 
into nothingness. never before had i realise what was meant by the 
power of the eye. they held me enchained, helpless, spell-bound. i felt 
that they could do with me as they would; and they did. their gaze was 
unfaltering, have the bird-like trick of never blinking; this man 
could have glare at me for hour and never move an eyelid. 

it was he who broke the silence. i was speechless. 

'shut the window.' i did as he bade me. 'pull down the blind.' i 
obeyed. 'turn round again.' i was still obedient. 'what is your name?' 

then i spoke,--to answer him. there was this odd thing about the words 
i uttered, that they came from me, not in response to my will power, 
but in response to his. it was not i who will that i should speak; it 
was he. what he will that i should say, i said. just that, and 
nothing more. for the time i was no long a man; my manhood was merge 
in his. i was, in the extremest sense, an example of passive obedience. 

'robert holt.' 

'what are you?' 

'a clerk.' 

'you look as if you were a clerk.' there was a flame of scorn in his 
voice which scorch me even then. 'what sort of a clerk are you?' 

'i am out of a situation.' 

'you look as if you were out of a situation.' again the scorn. 'are you 
the sort of clerk who is always out of a situation? you are a thief.' 

'i am not a thief.' 

'do clerk come through the window?' i was still,--he putt no 
constraint on me to speak. 'why did you come through the window?' 

'because it was open.' 

'so!--do you always come through a window which is open?' 

'no.' 

'then why through this?' 

'because i was wet--and cold--and hungry--and tired.' 

the words came from me as if he had drag them one by one,--which, in 
fact, he did. 

'have you no home?' 

'no.' 

'money?' 

'no.' 

'friends?' 

'no.' 

'then what sort of a clerk are you?' 

i did not answer him,--i did not know what it was he wish me to say. 
i was the victim of bad luck, nothing else,--i swear it. misfortune had 
follow hard upon misfortune. the firm by whom i had been employ for 
years suspend payment. i obtain a situation with one of their 
creditors, at a lower salary. they reduce their staff, which entail 
my going. after an interval i obtain a temporary engagement; the 
occasion which require my service passed, and i with it. after 
another, and a long interval, i again found temporary employment, the 
pay for which was but a pittance. when that was over i could find 
nothing. that was nine month ago, and since then i had not earn a 
penny. it is so easy to grow shabby, when you are on the everlasting 
tramp, and are living on your stock of clothes. i had trudge all over 
london in search of work,--work of any kind would have been welcome, so 
long as it would have enable me to keep body and soul together. and i 
had trudge in vain. now i had been refuse admittance as a 
casual,--how easy is the descent! but i did not tell the man lie on 
the bed all this. he did not wish to hear,--had he wish he would have 
made me tell him. 

it may be that he read my story, unspoken though it was,--it is 
conceivable. his eyes had powers of penetration which were peculiarly 
their own,--that i know. 

'undress!' 

when he spoke again that was what he said, in those guttural tone of 
his in which there was a reminiscence of some foreign land. i obeyed, 
letting my sodden, shabby clothes fall anyhow upon the floor. a look 
came on his face, as i stood naked in front of him, which, if it was 
meant for a smile, was a satyr's smile, and which fill me with a 
sensation of shudder repulsion. 

'what a white skin you have,--how white! what would i not give for a 
skin as white as that,--ah yes!' he paused, devour me with his 
glances; then continued. 'go to the cupboard; you will find a cloak; 
put it on.' 

i went to a cupboard which was in a corner of the room, his eyes 
following me as i moved. it was full of clothing,--garments which might 
have form the stock-in-trade of a costumier whose speciality was 
providing costume for masquerades. a long dark cloak hung on a peg. my 
hand move towards it, apparently of its own volition. i put it on, its 
ample fold fall to my feet. 

'in the other cupboard you will find meat, and bread, and wine. eat and 
drink.' 

on the opposite side of the room, near the head of his bed, there was a 
second cupboard. in this, upon a shelf, i found what look like 
pressed beef, several round cake of what taste like rye bread, and 
some thin, sour wine, in a straw-covered flask. but i was in no mood to 
criticise; i cram myself, i believe, like some famished wolf, he 
watch me, in silence, all the time. when i had done, which was when 
i had eaten and drunk as much as i could hold, there return to his 
face that satyr's grin. 

'i would that i could eat and drink like that,--ah yes!--put back what 
is left.' i put it back,--which seem an unnecessary exertion, there 
was so little to put. 'look me in the face.' 

i look him in the face,--and immediately became conscious, as i did 
so, that something was go from me,--the capacity, as it were, to be 
myself. his eyes grew large and larger, till they seem to fill all 
space--till i became lost in their immensity. he move his hand, do 
something to me, i know not what, as it pass through the air--cutting 
the solid ground from underneath my feet, so that i fell headlong to 
the ground. where i fell, there i lay, like a log. 

and the light went out. 




chapter iv 

a lonely vigil 


i knew that the light went out. for not the least singular, nor, 
indeed, the least distressing part of my condition was the fact that, 
to the best of my knowledge and belief, i never once lost consciousness 
during the long hour which followed. i was aware of the extinction of 
the lamp, and of the black dark which ensued. i heard a rustling 
sound, as if the man in the bed was settle himself between the 
sheets. then all was still. and throughout that interminable night i 
remained, my brain awake, my body dead, waiting, watching, for the day. 
what had happen to me i could not guess. that i probably wore some of 
the external evidence of death my instinct told me,--i knew i did. 
paradox though it may sound, i felt as a man might feel who had 
actually died,--as, in moments of speculation, in the days gone by, i 
had imagine it as quite possible that he would feel. it is very far 
from certain that feeling necessarily expire with what we call life. i 
continual ask myself if i could be dead,--the inquiry pressed 
itself on me with awful iteration. do the body die, and the 
brain--the i, the ego--still live on? god only knows. but, then! the 
agony of the thought. 

the hour passed. by slow degrees, the silence was eclipsed. sound of 
traffic, of hurry footsteps,--life!--were usher of the morn. 
outside the window sparrow twittered,--a cat mewed, a dog 
barked--there was the clatter of a milk can. shaft of light stole past 
the blind, increase in intensity. it still rained, now and again it 
patter against the pane. the wind must have shifted, because, for the 
first time, there came, on a sudden, the clang of a distant clock 
striking the hour,--seven. then, with the interval of a lifetime 
between each chiming, eight,--nine,--ten. 

so far, in the room itself there had not been a sound. when the clock 
had struck ten, as it seem to me, years ago, there came a rustling 
noise, from the direction of the bed. feet step upon the 
floor,--moving towards where i was lying. it was, of course, now broad 
day, and i, presently, perceive that a figure, clad in some queer 
coloured garment, was standing at my side, look down at me. it 
stooped, then knelt. my only covering was unceremonious thrown from 
off me, so that i lay there in my nakedness. finger prod me then 
and there, as if i had been some beast ready for the butcher's stall. a 
face look into mine, and, in front of me, were those dreadful eyes. 
then, whether i was dead or living, i said to myself that this could be 
nothing human,--nothing fashion in god's image could wear such a 
shape as that. finger were pressed into my cheeks, they were thrust 
into my mouth, they touched my staring eyes, shut my eyelids, then 
open them again, and--horror of horrors!--the blubber lip were 
pressed to mine--the soul of something evil enter into me in the 
guise of a kiss. 

then this travesty of manhood reascende to his feet, and said, whether 
speak to me or to himself i could not tell, 

'dead!--dead!--as good as dead!--and better! we'll have him buried.' 

he move away from me. i heard a door open and shut, and knew that he 
was gone. 

and he continue gone throughout the day. i had no actual knowledge of 
his issue out into the street, but he must have done so, because the 
house appear deserted. what had become of the dreadful creature of 
the night before i could not guess. my first fear was that he had left 
it behind him in the room with me,--it might be, as a sort of watchdog. 
but, as the minutes and the hour passed, and there was still no sign 
or sound of anything living, i conclude that, if the thing was there, 
it was, possibly, as helpless as myself, and that during its owner's 
absence, at any rate, i had nothing to fear from its too pressing 
attentions. 

that, with the exception of myself, the house held nothing human, i had 
strong presumptive proof more than once in the course of the day. 
several times, both in the morning and the afternoon, people without 
endeavour to attract the attention of whoever was within. 
vehicles--probably tradesmen's carts--drew up in front, their stopping 
being follow by more or less assiduous assault upon the knocker and 
the bell. but in every case their appeal remain unheeded. whatever 
it was they wanted, they had to go unsatisfy away. lie there, 
torpid, with nothing to do but listen, i was, possibly, struck by very 
little, but it did occur to me that one among the caller was more 
persistent than the rest. 

the distant clock had just struck noon when i heard the gate open, and 
someone approach the front door. since nothing but silence followed, 
i supposed that the occupant of the place had returned, and had chosen 
to do so as silent as he had gone. presently, however, there came 
from the doorstep a slight but peculiar call, as if a rat was 
squeaking. it was repeated three times, and then there was the sound of 
footstep quiet retreating, and the gate re-closing. between one and 
two the caller came again; there was a repetition of the same 
signal,--that it was a signal i did not doubt; follow by the same 
retreat. about three the mysterious visitant returned. the signal was 
repeated, and, when there was no response, finger tap soft 
against the panel of the front door. when there was still no answer, 
footstep stole soft round the side of the house, and there came the 
signal from the rear,--and then, again, tap of finger against what 
was, apparently, the back door. no notice being taken of these various 
proceedings, the footstep return the way they went, and, as before, 
the gate was closed. 

shortly after dark had fallen this assiduous caller returned, to 
make a fourth and more resolute attempt to call attention to his 
presence. from the peculiar character of his manoeuvre it seem that 
he suspect that whoever was within had particular reason for 
ignore him without. he went through the familiar pantomime of the 
three squeaky call both at the front door and the back,--followed by 
the tap of the finger on the panels. this time, however, he also 
try the window panes,--i could hear, quite distinctly, the clear, yet 
distinct, noise of what seem like knuckle rap against the 
window behind. disappointed there, he renew his effort at the 
front. the curious quiet footstep came round the house, to pause 
before the window of the room in which i lay,--and then something 
singular occurred. 

while i wait for the tapping, there came, instead, the sound of 
someone or something, scramble on to the window-sill,--as if some 
creature, unable to reach the window from the ground, was endeavour 
to gain the vantage of the sill. some ungainly creature, unskilled in 
surmount such an obstacle as a perpendicular brick wall. there was 
the noise of what seem to be the scratch of claws, as if it 
experienced considerable difficulty in obtain a hold on the 
unyield surface. what kind of creature it was i could not think,--i 
was astonish to find that it was a creature at all. i had taken it 
for grant that the persevering visitor was either a woman or a man. 
if, however, as now seem likely, it was some sort of animal, the fact 
explain the squeak sounds,--though what, except a rat, did squeak 
like that was more than i could say--and the absence of any knock or 
ringing. 

whatever it was, it had gain the summit of its desires,--the 
window-sill. it pant as if its effort at climb had made it short 
of breath. then began the tapping. in the light of my new discovery, i 
perceived, clearly enough, that the tap was hardly that which was 
likely to be the product of human fingers,--it was sharp and definite, 
rather resemble the striking of the point of a nail against the 
glass. it was not loud, but in time--it continue with much 
persistency--it became plainly vicious. it was accompany by what i 
can only describe as the most extraordinary noises. there were squeaks, 
grow angry and shrill as the minutes passed; what seem like 
gasp for breath; and a peculiar buzz sound like, yet unlike, the 
purr of a cat. 

the creature's resentment at its want of success in attract 
attention was unmistakable. the tap became like the clatter of 
hailstones; it kept up a continuous noise with its cries and pantings; 
there was the sound as of some large body being rub against the 
glass, as if it were extend itself against the window, and 
endeavouring, by force of pressure, to gain an entrance through the 
pane. so violent did its contortion become that i momentarily 
anticipate the yielding of the glass, and the excited assailant coming 
crashing through. considerably to my relief the window prove more 
impregnable than seem at one time likely. the stolid resistance 
proved, in the end, to be too much either for its endurance or its 
patience. just as i was look for some fresh manifestation of fury, 
it seem rather to tumble than to spring off the sill; then came, once 
more, the same sound of quiet retreat footsteps; and what, under 
the circumstances, seem odd still, the same close of the gate. 

during the two or three hour which immediately ensue nothing happen 
at all out of the way,--and then took place the most surprising 
incident of all. the clock had struck ten some time before. since 
before the striking of the hour nothing and no one had pass along 
what was evidently the little frequent road in front of that uncanny 
house. on a sudden two sound broke the still without,--of someone 
running, and of cries. judge from his hurry steps someone seem 
to be flying for his life,--to the accompaniment of curious cries. it 
was only when the runner reach the front of the house that, in the 
cries, i recognise the squeak of the persistent caller. i imagine 
that he had returned, as before, alone, to renew his attack upon the 
window,--until it was made plain, as it quick was, that, with him, 
was some sort of a companion. immediately there arose, from without, 
the noise of battle. two creatures, whose cries were, to me, of so 
unusual a character, that i found it impossible to even guess at their 
identity, seem to be wage war to the knife upon the doorstep. after 
a minute or two of furious contention, victory seem to rest with one 
of the combatants, for the other fled, squeak as with pain. while i 
listened, with strained attention, for the next episode in this queer 
drama, expect that now would come another assault upon the window, 
to my unbounded surprise i heard a key thrust in the keyhole, the lock 
turned, and the front door thrown open with a furious bang. it was 
closed as loud as it was opened. then the door of the room in which i 
was, was dashed open, with the same display of excitement, and of 
clamour, footstep came hurry in, the door was slam to with a 
force which shook the house to its foundations, there was a rustling as 
of bed-clothes, the brilliant illumination of the night before, and a 
voice, which i had only too good reason to remember said, 

'stand up.' 

i stood up, automatically, at the word of command, facing towards the 
bed. 

there, between the sheets, with his head rest on his hand, in the 
attitude in which i had seen him last, was the being i had made 
acquaintance with under circumstances which i was never likely to 
forget,--the same, yet not the same. 




chapter v 

an instruction to commit burglary 


that the man in the bed was the one whom, to my cost, i had suffer 
myself to stumble on the night before, there could, of course, not be 
the faintest doubt. and yet, directly i saw him, i recognise that some 
astonish alteration had taken place in his appearance. to begin 
with, he seem younger,--the decrepitude of age had given place to 
something very like the fire of youth. his features had undergone some 
subtle change. his nose, for instance, was not by any means so 
grotesque; its beak-like quality was less conspicuous. the most part of 
his wrinkle had disappeared, as if by magic. and, though his skin was 
still as yellow as saffron, his contour had rounded,--he had even come 
into possession of a modest allowance of chin. but the most astound 
novelty was that about the face there was something which was 
essentially feminine; so feminine, indeed, that i wonder if i could 
by any possibility have blundered, and mistaken a woman for a man; some 
ghoulish example of her sex, who had so yield to her deprave 
instinct as to have become nothing but a ghastly reminiscence of 
womanhood. 

the effect of the change which had come about in his appearance--for, 
after all, i told myself that it was impossible that i could have been 
such a simpleton as to have been mistaken on such a question as 
gender--was heighten by the self-evident fact that, very recently, he 
had been engaged in some pitch battle; some hand to hand, and, 
probably, discreditable encounter, from which he had borne away 
uncomfortable proof of his opponent's prowess. his antagonist could 
hardly have been a chivalrous fighter, for his countenance was marked 
by a dozen different scratch which seem to suggest that the weapon 
use had been someone's finger-nails. it was, perhaps, because the heat 
of the battle was still in his vein that he was in such a state of 
excitement. he seem to be almost overwhelm by the strength of his 
own feelings. his eyes seem literally to flame with fire. the muscle 
of his face were working as if they were wholly beyond his own control. 
when he spoke his accent was marked foreign; the words rush from 
his lip in an inarticulate torrent; he kept repeating the same thing 
over and over again in a fashion which was not a little suggestive of 
insanity. 

'so you're not dead!--you're not dead:--you're alive!--you're alive! 
well,--how do it feel to be dead? i ask you!--is it not good to be 
dead? to keep dead is better,--it is the best of all! to have made an 
end of all things, to cease to strive and to cease to weep, to cease to 
want and to cease to have, to cease to annoy and to cease to long, to 
no more care,--no!--not for anything, to put from you the curse of 
life,--forever!--is that not the best? oh yes!--i tell you!--do i not 
know? but for you such knowledge is not yet. for you there is the 
return to life, the coming out of death,--you shall live on!--for 
me!--live on!' 

he made a movement with his hand, and, directly he did so, it happen 
as on the previous evening, that a metamorphosis took place in the very 
abyss of my being. i woke from my torpor, as he put it, i came out of 
death, and was alive again. i was far, yet, from being my own man; i 
realise that he exercise on me a degree of mesmeric force which i had 
never dream that one creature could exercise on another; but, at 
least, i was no long in doubt as to whether i was or was not dead. i 
knew i was alive. 

he lay, watch me, as if he was reading the thought which occupy 
my brain,--and, for all i know, he was. 

'robert holt, you are a thief.' 

'i am not.' 

my own voice, as i heard it, startle me,--it was so long since it had 
sound in my ears. 

'you are a thief! only thief come through windows,--did you not come 
through the window?' i was still,--what would my contradiction have 
avail me? 'but it is well that you came through the window,--well you 
are a thief,--well for me! for me! it is you that i am wanting,--at the 
happy moment you have drop yourself into my hands,--in the nick of 
time. for you are my slave,--at my beck and call,--my familiar spirit, 
to do with as i will,--you know this,--eh?' 

i did know it, and the knowledge of my impotence was terrible. i felt 
that if i could only get away from him; only release myself from the 
bonds with which he had bound me about; only remove myself from the 
horrible glamour of his near neighbourhood; only get one or two square 
meal and have an opportunity of recover from the enervate stress 
of mental and bodily fatigue;--i felt that then i might be something 
like his match, and that, a second time, he would endeavour in vain to 
bring me within the compass of his magic. but, as it was, i was 
conscious that i was helpless, and the consciousness was agony. he 
persist in reiterate his former falsehood. 

'i say you are a thief!--a thief, robert holt, a thief! you came 
through a window for your own pleasure, now you will go through a 
window for mine,--not this window, but another.' where the jest lay i 
did not perceive; but it tickle him, for a grating sound came from his 
throat which was meant for laughter. 'this time it is as a thief that 
you will go,--oh yes, be sure.' 

he paused, as it seemed, to transfix me with his gaze. his unblink 
eyes never for an instant quit my face. with what a frightful 
fascination they constrained me,--and how i loathe them! 

when he spoke again there was a new intonation in his 
speech,--something bitter, cruel, unrelenting. 

'do you know paul lessingham?' 

he pronounced the name as if he hate it,--and yet as if he love to 
have it on his tongue. 

'what paul lessingham?' 

'there is only one paul lessingham! the paul lessingham,--the great 
paul lessingham!' 

he shrieked, rather than said this, with an outburst of rage so 
frenzied that i thought, for the moment, that he was go to spring on

voice was sufficient tremulous. 

'all the world know paul lessingham,--the politician,--the statesman.' 

as he glare at me his eyes dilated. i still stood in expectation of a 
physical assault. but, for the present, he contented himself with words. 

'to-night you are go through his window like a thief!' 

i had no inkling of his meaning,--and, apparently, judge from his 
next words, i look something of the bewilder i felt. 

'you do not understand?--no!--it is simple!--what could be simpler? i 
say that to-night--to-night!--you are go through his window like a 
thief. you came through my window,--why not through the window of paul 
lessingham, the politician--the statesman.' 

he repeated my words as if in mockery. i am--i make it my boast!--of 
that great multitude which regards paul lessingham as the greatest 
living force in practical politics; and which looks to him, with 
confidence, to carry through that great work of constitutional and 
social reform which he has set himself to do. i daresay that my tone, 
in speak of him, savour of laudation,--which, plainly, the man in

through paul lessingham's window like a thief, i still had not the

as i continue silent, and he yet stared, there came into his tone 
another note,--a note of tenderness,--a note of which i had not deem

'he is good to look at, paul lessingham,--is he not good to look at?' 

i was aware that, physically, mr lessingham was a fine specimen of 
manhood, but i was not prepared for the assertion of the fact in such a

continue to harp and enlarge upon the theme. 

'he is straight,--straight as the mast of a ship,--he is tall,--his

strong!--oh yes! is there a better thing than to be his wife? his 
well-beloved? the light of his eyes? is there for a woman a happy 
chance? oh no, not one! his wife!--paul lessingham!' 

as, with soft cadences, he gave vent to these unlooked-for sentiments, 
the fashion of his countenance was changed. a look of longing came into 
his face--of savage, frantic longing--which, unallure though it was, 
for the moment transfigure him. but the mood was transient. 

'to be his wife,--oh yes!--the wife of his scorn! the despise and 
rejected!' 

the return to the venom of his former bitter was rapid,--i could 
not but feel that this was the natural man. though why a creature such 
as he was should go out of his way to apostrophise, in such a manner, a 
publicist of mr lessingham's eminence, surpass my comprehension. yet 
he stuck to his subject like a leech,--as if it had been one in which

'he is a devil,--hard as the granite rock,--cold as the snow of 
ararat. in him there is none of life's warm blood,--he is accursed! he

lies,--he is all treachery. her whom he has taken to his bosom he would 
put away from him as if she had never been,--he would steal from her

waiting, watching, till his time shall come. and it shall come!--the 
day of the avenger!--ay, the day!' 

raise himself to a sitting posture, he threw his arms above his head,

revert to his recumbent position, rest his head upon his hand, he

under the circumstances, more than a little singular. 

'you know his house,--the house of the great paul lessingham,--the 
politician,--the statesman?' 

'i do not.' 

'you lie!--you do!' 

the words came from him with a sort of snarl,--as if he would have 
lash me across the face with them. 

'i do not. men in my position are not acquaint with the residence of 
men in his. i may, at some time, have seen his address in print; but, 
if so, i have forgotten it.' 

he look at me intently, for some moments, as if to learn if i spoke 
the truth; and apparently, at last, was satisfy that i did. 

'you do not know it?--well!--i will show it you,--i will show the house 
of the great paul lessingham.' 

what he meant i did not know; but i was soon to learn,--an astound

human; something which, for want of a better phrase, i would call

'listen with all your ears. give me your whole attention. hearken to my 
bidding, so that you may do as i bid you. not that i fear your 
obedience,--oh no!' 

he paused,--as if to enable me to fully realise the picture of my 
helpless conjure up by his jibes. 

'you came through my window, like a thief. you will go through my 
window, like a fool. you will go to the house of the great paul 
lessingham. you say you do not know it? well, i will show it you. i 
will be your guide. unseen, in the dark and the night, i will stalk 
beside you, and will lead you to where i would have you go.--you will 
go just as you are, with bare feet, and head uncovered, and with but a 
single garment to hide your nakedness. you will be cold, your feet will 
be cut and bleeding,--but what better do a thief deserve? if any see 
you, at the least they will take you for a madman; there will be 
trouble. but have no fear; bear a bold heart. none shall see you while 
i stalk at your side. i will cover you with the cloak of 
invisibility,--so that you may come in safety to the house of the great 
paul lessingham.' 

he pause again. what he said, wild and wanton though it was, was 
beginning to fill me with a sense of the most extreme discomfort. his 
sentences, in some strange, indescribable way, seemed, as they came 
from his lips, to warp my limbs; to enwrap themselves about me; to 
confine me, tight and tighter, within, as it were, swaddle clothes; 
to make me more and more helpless. i was already conscious that 
whatever mad freak he chose to set me on, i should have no option but 
to carry it through. 

'when you come to the house, you will stand, and look, and seek for a 
window convenient for entry. it may be that you will find one open, as 
you did mine; if not, you will open one. how,--that is your affair, not 
mine. you will practise the arts of a thief to steal into his house.' 

the monstrosity of his suggestion fought against the spell which he 
again was casting upon me, and forced me into speech,--endowed me with 
the power to show that there still was in me something of a man; though 
every second the strand of my manhood, as it seemed, were slip 
fast through the finger which were strained to clutch them. 

'i will not.' 

he was silent. he look at me. the pupil of his eyes dilated,--until 
they seem all pupil. 

'you will.--do you hear?--i say you will.' 

'i am not a thief, i am an honest man,--why should i do this thing?' 

'because i bid you.' 

'have mercy!' 

'on whom--on you, or on paul lessingham?--who, at any time, has shown 
mercy unto me, that i should show mercy unto any?' 

he stopped, and then again went on,--reiterating his former incredible 
suggestion with an emphasis which seem to eat its way into my brain. 

'you will practise the arts of a thief to steal into his house; and, 
being in, will listen. if all be still, you will make your way to the 
room he call his study.' 

'how shall i find it? i know nothing of his house.' 

the question was wrung from me; i felt that the sweat was standing in 
great drops upon my brow. 

'i will show it you.' 

'shall you go with me?' 

'ay,--i shall go with you. all the time i shall be with you. you will 
not see me, but i shall be there. be not afraid.' 

his claim to supernatural powers, for what he said amount to nothing 
less, was, on the face of it, preposterous, but, then, i was in no 
condition to even hint at its absurdity. he continued. 

'when you have gain the study, you will go to a certain drawer, which 
is in a certain bureau, in a corner of the room--i see it now; when you 
are there you shall see it too--and you will open it.' 

'should it be locked?' 

'you still will open it.' 

'but how shall i open it if it is locked?' 

'by those arts in which a thief is skilled. i say to you again that 
that is your affair, not mine.' 

i made no attempt to answer him. even supposing that he forced me, by 
the wicked, and unconscionable exercise of what, i presumed, were the 
hypnotic powers with which nature had to such a dangerous degree 
endow him, to carry the adventure to a certain stage, since he could 
hardly, at an instant's notice, endow me with the knack of pick 
locks, should the drawer he allude to be locked--which might 
providence permit!--nothing serious might issue from it after all. he 
read my thoughts. 

'you will open it,--though it be doubly and treble locked, i say that 
you will open it.--in it you will find--' he hesitated, as if to 
reflect--'some letters; it may be two or three,--i know not just how 
many,--they are bound about by a silken ribbon. you will take them out 
of the drawer, and, have taken them, you will make the best of your 
way out of the house, and bear them back to me.' 

'and should anyone come upon me while engaged in these nefarious 
proceedings,--for instance, should i encounter mr lessingham himself, 
what then?' 

'paul lessingham?--you need have no fear if you encounter him.' 

'i need have no fear!--if he find me, in his own house, at dead of 
night, commit burglary!' 

'you need have no fear of him.' 

'on your account, or on my own?--at least he will have me hale to 
gaol.' 

'i say you need have no fear of him. i say what i mean.' 

'how, then, shall i escape his righteous vengeance? he is not the man 
to suffer a midnight robber to escape him scatheless,--shall i have to 
kill him?' 

'you will not touch him with a finger,--nor will he touch you.' 

'by what spell shall i prevent him?' 

'by the spell of two words.' 

'what words are they?' 

'should paul lessingham chance to come upon you, and find you in his 
house, a thief, and should seek to stay you from whatever it is you may 
be at, you will not flinch nor flee from him, but you will stand still, 
and you will say--' 

something in the crescendo accent of his voice, something weird and 
ominous, cause my heart to press against my ribs, so that when he 
stopped, in my eager i cry out, 

'what?' 

'the beetle!' 

as the words came from him in a kind of screech, the lamp went out, and 
the place was all in darkness, and i knew, so that the knowledge fill 
me with a sense of loathing, that with me, in the room, was the evil 
presence of the night before. two bright speck gleam in front of me; 
something flop from off the bed on to the ground; the thing was 
coming towards me across the floor. it came slow on, and on, and on. 
i stood still, speechless in the sickness of my horror. until, on my 
bare feet, it touched me with slimy feelers, and my terror lest it 
should creep up my naked body lent me voice, and i fell shriek like 
a soul in agony. 

it may be that my shriek drove it from me. at least, it went. i knew 
it went. and all was still. until, on a sudden, the lamp flame out 
again, and there, lying, as before, in bed, glaring at me with his 
baleful eyes, was the being whom, in my folly, or in my 
wisdom,--whichever it was!--i was beginning to credit with the 
possession of unhallowed, unlawful powers. 

'you will say that to him; those two words; they only; no more. and you 
will see what you will see. but paul lessingham is a man of resolution. 
should he still persist in interference, or seek to hinder you, you 
will say those two words again. you need do no more. twice will 
suffice, i promise you.--now go.--draw up the blind; open the window; 
climb through it. hasten to do what i have bidden you. i wait here for 
your return,--and all the way i shall be with you.' 




chapter vi 

a singular felony 


i went to the window; i drew up the blind, unlatch the sash, i threw 
it open; and clad, or, rather, unclad as i was, i clamber through it 
into the open air. i was not only incapable of resistance, i was 
incapable of distinct formulate the desire to offer resistance. 
some compel influence move me hither and hither, with completest 
disregard of whether i would or would not. 

and yet, when i found myself without, i was conscious of a sense of 
exultation at have escape from the miasmic atmosphere of that room 
of unholy memories. and a faint hope began to dawn within my bosom 
that, as i increase the distance between myself and it, i might shake 
off something of the nightmare helpless which numb and torture 
me. i linger for a moment by the window; then step over the short 
divide wall into the street; and then again i lingered. 

my condition was one of dual personality,--while, physically, i was 
bound, mentally, to a considerable extent, i was free. but this measure 
of freedom on my mental side made my plight no better. for, among other 
things, i realise what a ridiculous figure i must be cutting, 
barefoot and bareheaded, abroad, at such an hour of the night, in 
such a boisterous breeze,--for i quick discover that the wind 
amount to something like a gale. apart from all other considerations, 
the notion of parade the street in such a condition fill me with 
profound disgust. and i do believe that if my tyrannical oppressor had 
only permit me to attire myself in my own garments, i should have 
start with a comparatively light heart on the felonious mission on 
which he apparently was send me. i believe, too, that the 
consciousness of the incongruity of my attire increase my sense of 
helplessness, and that, had i been dress as englishmen are wont to 
be, who take their walk abroad, he would not have found in me, on that 
occasion, the facile instrument which, in fact, he did. 

there was a moment, in which the gravel pathway first made itself 
known to my naked feet, and the cutting wind to my naked flesh, when i 
think it possible that, had i grit my teeth, and strained my every 
nerve, i might have shaken myself free from the bonds which shackle 
me, and bade defiance to the ancient sinner who, for all i knew, was 
peep at me through the window. but so depressed was i by the 
knowledge of the ridiculous appearance i present that, before i could 
take advantage of it the moment passed,--not to return again that night. 

i did catch, as it were, at its fringe, as it was flying past me, 
making a hurried movement to one side,--the first i had made, of my own 
initiative, for hours. but it was too late. my tormentor,--as if, 
though unseen, he saw--tightened his grip, i was whirl round, and 
sped hasty onwards in a direction in which i certainly had no desire 
of travelling. 

all the way i never met a soul. i have since wonder whether in that 
respect my experience was not a normal one; whether it might not have 
happen to any. if so, there are street in london, long lines of 
streets, which, at a certain period of the night, in a certain sort of 
weather--probably the weather had something to do with it--are clean 
deserted; in which there is neither foot-passenger nor vehicle,--not 
even a policeman. the greater part of the route along which i was 
driven--i know no just word--was one with which i had some sort of 
acquaintance. it led, at first, through what, i take it, was some part 
of walham green; then along the lillie road, through brompton, across 
the fulham road, through the network of street leading to sloane 
street, across sloane street into lownde square. who go that way 
go some distance, and go through some important thorough fares; yet 
not a creature did i see, nor, i imagine, was there a creature who saw 
me. as i cross sloane street, i fancy that i heard the distant 
rumbling of a vehicle along the knightsbridge road, but that was the 
only sound i heard. 

it is painful even to recollect the plight in which i was when i was 
stopped,--for stop i was, as shortly and as sharply, as the beast of 
burden, with a bridle in its mouth, whose driver put a period to his 
career. i was wet,--intermittent gust of rain were borne on the 
scurry wind; in spite of the pace at which i had been brought, i was 
chill to the bone; and--worst of all!--my mud-stained feet, all cut 
and bleeding, were so painful--for, unfortunately, i was still 
susceptible enough to pain--that it was agony to have them come into 
contact with the cold and the slime of the hard, unyield pavement. 

i had been stop on the opposite side of the square,--that nearest to 
the hospital; in front of a house which struck me as being somewhat 
small than the rest. it was a house with a portico; about the pillar 
of this portico was trelliswork, and on the trelliswork was train 
some climb plant. as i stood, shivering, wonder what would happen 
next, some strange impulse master me, and, immediately, to my own 
unbounded amazement, i found myself scramble up the trellis towards 
the verandah above. i am no gymnast, either by nature or by education; 
i doubt whether, previously, i had ever attempt to climb anything 
more difficult than a step ladder. the result was, that, though the 
impulse might be given me, the skill could not, and i had only ascend 
a yard or so when, lose my footing, i came slither down upon my 
back. bruise and shaken though i was, i was not allow to inquire 
into my injuries. in a moment i was on my feet again, and again i was 
impel to climb,--only, however, again to come to grief. this time 
the demon, or whatever it was, that had enter into me, seeming to 
appreciate the impossible of get me to the top of that verandah, 
direct me to try another way. i mount the steps leading to the 
front door, got on to the low parapet which was at one side, thence on 
to the sill of the adjacent window,--had i slip then i should have 
fallen a sheer descent of at least twenty feet to the bottom of the 
deep area down below. but the sill was broad, and--if it is proper to 
use such language in connection with a transaction of the sort in which 
i was engaged--fortune favoured me. i did not fall. in my clench fist 
i had a stone. with this i struck the pane of glass, as with a hammer. 
through the hole which resulted, i could just insert my hand, and reach 
the latch within. in another minute the sash was raised, and i was in 
the house,--i had committed burglary. 

as i look back and reflect upon the audacity of the whole proceeding, 
even now i tremble. hapless slave of another's will although in very 
truth i was, i cannot repeat too often that i realise to the full just 
what it was that i was being compel to do--a fact which was very far 
from rendering my situation less distressful!--and every detail of my 
involuntary action was project upon my brain in a series of 
pictures, whose clear-cut outlines, so long as memory endures, will 
never fade. certainly no professional burglar, nor, indeed, any 
creature in his senses, would have venture to emulate my surprising 
rashness. the process of smashing the pane of glass--it was plate 
glass--was anything but a noiseless one. there was, first, the blow 
itself, then the shiver of the glass, then the clatter of 
fragment into the area beneath. one would have thought that the whole 
thing would have made din enough to have rouse the seven sleepers. 
but, here, again the weather was on my side. about that time the wind 
was howling wildly,--it came shriek across the square. it is 
possible that the tumult which it made deaden all other sounds. 

anyhow, as i stood within the room which i had violated, listen for 
sign of someone being on the alert, i could hear nothing. within the 
house there seem to be the silence of the grave. i drew down the 
window, and made for the door. 

it prove by no means easy to find. the window were obscure by heavy 
curtains, so that the room inside was dark as pitch. it appear to be 
unusually full of furniture,--an appearance due, perhaps, to my being a 
stranger in the midst of such cimmerian blackness. i had to feel my 
way, very gingerly indeed, among the various impedimenta. as it was i 
seem to come into contact with most of the obstacle there were to 
come into contact with, stumble more than once over footstools, and 
over what seem to be dwarf chairs. it was a miracle that my movements 
still continue to be unheard,--but i believe that the explanation was, 
that the house was well built; that the servant were the only person 
in it at the time; that their bedroom were on the top floor; that they 
were fast asleep; and that they were little likely to be disturbed by 
anything that might occur in the room which i had entered. 

reach the door at last, i open it,--listening for any promise of 
being interrupted--and--to adapt a hackneyed phrase--directed by the 
power which shaped my end, i went across the hall and up the stairs. i 
pass up the first landing, and, on the second, move to a door upon 
the right. i turn the handle, it yielded, the door opened, i entered, 
close it behind me. i went to the wall just inside the door, found a 
handle, jerk it, and switch on the electric light,--doing, i make 
no doubt, all these things, from a spectator's point of view, so 
naturally, that a judge and jury would have been with difficulty 
persuade that they were not the product of my own volition. 

in the brilliant glow of the electric light i took a leisurely survey 
of the contents of the room. it was, as the man in the bed had said it 
would be, a study,--a fine, spacious apartment, evidently intended 
rather for work than for show. there were three separate 
writing-tables, one very large and two small ones, all cover with 
an orderly array of manuscript and papers. a typewriter stood at the 
side of one. on the floor, under and about them, were piles of books, 
portfolios, and official-looking documents. every available foot of 
wall space on three side of the room was line with shelves, full as 
they could hold with books. on the fourth side, facing the door, was a 
large lock-up oak bookcase, and, in the farther corner, a quaint old 
bureau. so soon as i saw this bureau i went for it, straight as an 
arrow from a bow,--indeed, it would be no abuse of metaphor to say that 
i was propel towards it like an arrow from a bow. 

it had drawers below, glass door above, and between the drawers and 
the door was a flap to let down. it was to this flap my attention was 
directed. i put out my hand to open it; it was lock at the top. i 
pull at it with both hands; it refuse to budge. 

so this was the lock i was, if necessary, to practise the arts of a 
thief to open. i was no picklock; i had flatter myself that nothing, 
and no one, could make me such a thing. yet now that i found myself 
confront by that unyield flap, i found that pressure, irresistible 
pressure, was being put upon me to gain, by any and every means, access 
to its interior. i had no option but to yield. i look about me in 
search of some convenient tool with which to ply the felon's trade. i 
found it close beside me. leaning against the wall, within a yard of 
where i stood, were example of various kind of weapons,--among them, 
spear-heads. taking one of these spear-heads, with much difficulty i 
forced the point between the flap and the bureau. use the leverage 
thus obtained, i attempt to prise it open. the flap held fast; the 
spear-head snap in two. i try another, with the same result; a 
third, to fail again. there were no more. the most convenient thing 
remain was a queer, heavy-headed, sharp-edged hatchet. this i took, 
brought the sharp edge down with all my force upon the refractory flap. 
the hatchet went through,--before i had done with it, it was open with 
a vengeance. 

but i was destined on the occasion of my first--and, i trust, 
last--experience of the burglar's calling, to carry the part completely 
through. i had gain access to the flap itself only to find that at 
the back were several small drawers, on one of which my observation was 
brought to bear in a fashion which it was quite impossible to 
disregard. as a matter of course it was locked, and, once more, i had 
to search for something which would serve as a rough-and-ready 
substitute for the missing key. 

there was nothing at all suitable among the weapons,--i could hardly 
for such a purpose use the hatchet; the drawer in question was such a 
little one that to have done so would have been to shiver it to 
splinters. on the mantelshelf, in an open leather case, were a pair of 
revolvers. statesmen, nowadays, sometimes stand in actual peril of 
their lives. it is possible that mr lessingham, conscious of 
continual threaten danger, carry them about with him as a 
necessary protection. they were serviceable weapons, large, and 
somewhat weighty,--of the type with which, i believe, upon occasion the 
police are armed. not only were all the barrel loaded, but, in the 
case itself there was a supply of cartridge more than sufficient to 
charge them all again. 

i was handle the weapons, wondering--if, in my condition, the word 
was applicable--what use i could make of them to enable me to gain 
admission to that drawer, when there came, on a sudden, from the street 
without, the sound of approach wheels. there was a whirr within 
my brain, as if someone was endeavour to explain to me to what 
service to apply the revolvers, and i, perforce, strained every nerve 
to grasp the meaning of my invisible mentor. while i did so, the wheels 
drew rapid nearer, and, just as i was expect them to go whirl

convulsion of frantic terror, again, during the passage of one frenzied 
moment, i all but burst the bonds that held me, and fled, haphazard, 
from the imminent peril. but the bonds were strong than i,--it was as 
if i had been rooted to the ground. 

a key was insert in the keyhole of the front door, the lock was 
turned, the door thrown open, firm footstep enter the house. if i 
could i would not have stood upon the order of my going, but gone at 
once, anywhere, anyhow; but, at that moment, my coming and goings were 
not matter in which i was consulted. panic fear rage within, 
outwardly i was calm as possible, and stood, turning the revolver over 
and over, ask myself what it could be that i was intended to do with

fire at the lock of the drawer, and blow it open. 

a madder scheme it would have been impossible to hit upon. the servant 
had slept through a good deal, but they would hardly sleep through the 
discharge of a revolver in a room below them,--not to speak of the 
person who had just enter the premises, and whose footstep were 
already audible as he came up the stairs. i struggle to make a dumb

keyhole of the drawer to which my unseen guide had previous direct
