to believe is paul lessingham's has received its quietus; in the 
morning i'll send it back to him, with my respectful compliments. he'll 
miss it if i don't.--reflect! think of a huge bomb, fill with what 
we'll call atherton's magic vapour, fired, say, from a hundred and 
twenty ton gun, burst at a given elevation over the head of an 
oppose force. properly managed, in less than an instant of time, a 
hundred thousand men,--quite possibly more!--would drop down dead, as 
if smitten by the lightning of the skies. isn't that something like a 
weapon, sir?' 

'i'm not well!--i want to get away!--i wish i'd never come!' 

that was all woodville had to say. 

'rubbish!--you're add to your stock of information every second, 
and, in these days, when a member of parliament is supposed to know all 
about everything, information's the one thing wanted. empty your glass, 
man,--that's the time of day for you!' 

i hand him his tumbler. he drain what was left of its contents, 
then, in a fit of tipsy, childish temper he flung the tumbler from him. 
i had placed--carelessly enough--the second pellet within a foot of the

the door open, i got him into the open air. 

as i did so, i found myself confront by someone who stood outside. it 
was lessingham's mysterious egypto-arabian friend,--my morning's 
visitor. 




chapter xvii 

magic?--or miracle? 


the passage into the yard from the electrical lit laboratory was a 
passage from brilliancy to gloom. the shroud figure, standing in the

only because i had resolute held my breath, and kept my face avert

exclaiming,--remembering the saying about the engineer being hoist by 
his own petard, 

'atherton's magic vapour!' 

my sensation on return to consciousness were curious. i found

look into mine. 

'who the deuce are you?' i asked. 

then, understanding that it was my uninvited visitor, with scant 
ceremony i drew myself away from him. by the light which was stream 
through the laboratory door i saw that woodville was lie close beside 
me,--stark and still. 

'is he dead?' i cried. 'percy.--speak, man!--it's not so bad with you 
as that!' 

but it was pretty bad,--so bad that, as i bent down and look at him, 
my heart beat uncomfortable fast lest it was as bad as it could be. his 
heart seem still,--the vapour took effect directly on the cardiac 
centres. to revive their action and that instantly, was indispensable. 
yet my brain was in such a whirl that i could not even think of how to 
set about beginning. had i been alone, it is more than probable

the stranger, passing his arms beneath his body, extend himself at

the way in which his teeth and the whites of his eyes were exposed,

the stranger must have seen what was passing through my mind,--not a 
very difficult thing to see. point to the recumbent percy, he said, 
with that queer foreign twang of his, which, whatever it had seem 
like in the morning, sound musical enough just then. 

'all will be well with him.' 

'i am not so sure.' 

the stranger did not deign to answer. he was kneel on one side of 
the victim of modern science, i on the other. passing his hand to and 
fro in front of the unconscious countenance, as if by magic all 
semblance of discomfort vanish from percy's features, and, to all 
appearances, he was placid asleep. 

'have you hypnotise him?' 

'what do it matter?' 

if it was a case of hypnotism, it was very neat done. the conditions 
were both unusual and trying, the effect produce seem all that could 
be desired,--the change brought about in half a dozen seconds was quite 
remarkable. i began to be aware of a feeling of quasi-respect for paul 
lessingham's friend. his morals might be peculiar, and manners he might 
have none, but in this case, at any rate, the end seem to have 
justified the means. he went on. 

'he sleeps. when he awake he will remember nothing that has been. 
leave him,--the night is warm,--all will be well.' 

as he said, the night was warm,--and it was dry. percy would come to 
little harm by being allow to enjoy, for a while, the pleasant 
breezes. so i act on the stranger's advice, and left him lie in the 
yard, while i had a little interview with the impromptu physician. 




chapter xviii 

the apotheosis of the beetle 


the laboratory door was closed. the stranger was standing a foot or two 
away from it. i was further within the room, and was subject him to 
as keen a scrutiny as circumstances permitted. beyond doubt he was 
conscious of my observation, yet he bore himself with an air of 
indifference, which was suggestive of perfect unconcern. the fellow was 
oriental to the finger-tips,--that much was certain; yet in spite of a 
pretty wide personal knowledge of oriental people i could not make up 
my mind as to the exact part of the east from which he came. he was 
hardly an arab, he was not a fellah,--he was not, unless i erred, a 
mohammedan at all. there was something about him which was distinct 
not mussulmanic. so far as looks were concerned, he was not a 
flatter example of his race, whatever his race might be. the 
portentous size of his beak-like nose would have been, in itself, 
sufficient to damn him in any court of beauty. his lip were thick and 
shapeless,--and this, join to another peculiarity in his appearance, 
seem to suggest that, in his vein there ran more than a streak of 
negro blood. the peculiarity allude to was his semblance of great age. 
as one eye him one was remind of the legend told of people who have 
been supposed to have retain something of their pristine vigour after 
have live for centuries. as, however, one continue to gaze, one 
began to wonder if he really was so old as he seemed,--if, indeed, he 
was exceptional old at all. negroes, and especially negresses, are 
apt to age with extreme rapidity. among coloured folk one sometimes 
encounter women whose face seem to have been line by the passage of 
centuries, yet whose actual tale of years would entitle them to regard 
themselves, here in england, as in the prime of life. the senility of 
the fellow's countenance, besides, was contradict by the juvenescence

curious shaped, remind me of the elongated, facet eyes of some

force and fire, but, also, with the frenzy of youth. more

signal never yet were placed in a creature's head. the individual who, 
have once caught sight of him, still sought to cultivate their 
owner's acquaintance, had only himself to thank if the very worst 
result of frequent evil company prompt ensued. 

it happen that i am myself endow with an unusual tenacity of vision. 
i could, for instance, easily outstare any man i ever met. yet, as i

'what is it i am being warn against,--the beetle?' 

'yes,--the beetle!' 

were i upon oath, and this statement being made, in the presence of 
witnesses, say, in a solicitor's office, i standing in fear of pains 
and penalties, i think that, at this point, i should leave the paper 
blank. no man likes to own himself a fool, or that he ever was a 
fool,--and ever since i have been wonder whether, on that occasion, 
that 'child of isis' did, or did not, play the fool with me. his 
performance was realistic enough at the time, heaven knows. but, as it 
get farther and farther away, i ask myself, more and more confidently, 
as time effluxes, whether, after all, it was not clever 
juggling,--superhumanly clever juggling, if you will; that, and nothing 
more. if it was something more, then, with a vengeance! there is more 
in heaven and earth than is dream of in our philosophy. the mere 
possibility open vista which the sane mind fear to contemplate. 

since, then, i am not on oath, and, should i fall short of verbal 
accuracy, i do not need to fear the engine of the law, what seem to 
happen was this. 

he was standing within about ten feet of where i lean against the 
edge of the table. the light was full on, so that it was difficult to 
suppose that i could make a mistake as to what took place in front of 
me. as he reply to my mock allusion to the beetle by echo my 
own words, he vanished,--or, rather, i saw him taking a different shape 
before my eyes. his loose drapery all fell off him, and, as they were 
in the very act of falling, there issued, or there seem to issue out 
of them, a monstrous creature of the beetle type,--the man himself was 
gone. on the point of size i wish to make myself clear. my impression, 
when i saw it first, was that it was as large as the man had been, and 
that it was, in some way, standing up on end, the leg towards me. but, 
the moment it came in view, it began to dwindle, and that so rapid 
that, in a couple of seconds at most, a little heap of drapery was 
lie on the floor, on which was a truly astonish example of the 
coleoptera. it appear to be a beetle. it was, perhaps, six or seven 
inch high, and about a foot in length. its scale were of a vivid 
golden green. i could distinct see where the wings were sheathe 
along the back, and, as they seem to be slightly agitated, i looked, 
every moment, to see them opened, and the thing take wing. 

i was so astonished,--as who would not have been?--that for an 
appreciable space of time i was practically in a state of stupefaction. 
i could do nothing but stare. i was acquaint with the legendary 
transmigration of isis, and with the story of the beetle which issue 
from the woman's womb through all eternity, and with the other pretty 
tales, but this, of which i was an actual spectator, was something new, 
even in legends. if the man, with whom i had just been speaking, was 
gone, where had he gone to? if this glittering creature was there, in 
his stead, whence had it come? 

i do protest this much, that, after the first shock of surprise had 
passed, i retain my presence of mind. i felt as an investigate might 
feel, who has stumbled, haphazard, on some astounding, some 
epoch-making, discovery. i was conscious that i should have to make the 
best use of my mental faculty if i was to take full advantage of so 
astonish an accident. i kept my glance rivet on the creature, with 
the idea of photograph it on my brain. i believe that if it were 
possible to take a retinal print--which it someday will be--you would 
have a perfect picture of what it was i saw, beyond doubt it was a 
lamellicorn, one of the copridae. with the one exception of its 
monstrous size, there were the characteristic in plain view;--the 
convex body, the large head, the project clypeus. more, its smooth 
head and throat seem to suggest that it was a female. equally beyond 
a doubt, apart from its size, there were unusual features present too. 
the eyes were not only unwonted conspicuous, they gleam as if they 
were light by internal flames,--in some indescribable fashion they 
remind me of my vanish visitor. the colouring was superb, and the 
creature appear to have the chameleon-like faculty of lighten and 
darken the shades at will. its not least curious feature was its 
restlessness. it was in a state of continual agitation; and, as if it 
resent my inspection, the more i look at it the more its agitation 
grew. as i have said, i expect every moment to see it take wing and 
circle through the air. 

all the while i was casting about in my mind as to what means i could 
use to effect its capture. i did think of killing it, and, on the 
whole, i rather wish that i had at any rate attempt slaughter,--there 
were dozen of things, lie ready to my hand, any one of which would 
have severe try its constitution;--but, on the spur of the moment, 
the only method of taking it alive which occur to me, was to pop 
over it a big tin canister which had contained soda-lime. this canister 
was on the floor to my left. i move towards it, as nonchalant as i 
could, keeping an eye on that shining wonder all the time. directly i 
moved, its agitation perceptible increased,--it was, so to speak, all 
one whirr of tremblement; it scintillated, as if its coloured scale 
had been so many prisms; it began to unsheath its wings, as if it had 
finally decided that it would make use of them. pick up the tin, 
disembarrass it of its lid, i sprang towards my intended victim. its 
wings open wide; obviously it was about to rise; but it was too late. 
before it had clear the ground, the tin was over it. 

it remain over it, however, for an instant only. i had stumbled, in 
my haste, and, in my effort to save myself from fall face foremost 
on to the floor, i was compel to remove my hand from the tin. 
before i was able to replace them, the tin was sent flying, and, while 
i was still partially recumbent, within eighteen inch of me, that 
beetle swell and swelled, until it had assume its former portentous 
dimensions, when, as it seemed, it was envelop by a human shape, and 
in less time than no time, there stood in front of me, naked from top 
to toe, my truly versatile oriental friend. one startle fact nudity 
revealed,--that i had been egregious mistaken on the question of sex. 
my visitor was not a man, but a woman, and, judge from the brief 
glimpse which i had of her body, by no means old or ill-shaped either. 

if that transformation was not a bewilder one, then two and two make 
five. the most level-headed scientist would temporary have lost his 
mental equipoise on witness such a quick change as that within a 
span or two of his own nose i was not only witless, i was breathless 
too,--i could only gape. and, while i gaped, the woman, stoop down, 
pick up her draperies, began to huddle them on her anyhow,--and, 
also, to skeddadle towards the door which led into the yard. when i 
observe this last manoeuvre, to some extent i did rise to the 
requirement of the situation. leap up, i rush to stay her flight. 

'stop!' i shouted. 

but she was too quick for me. ere i could reach her, she had open the 
door, and was through it,--and, what was more, she had slam it in my 
face. in my excitement, i did some fumble with the handle. when, in 
my turn, i was in the yard, she was out of sight. i did fancy i saw a 
dim form disappear over the wall at the further side, and i made for 
it as fast as i knew how. i clamber on to the wall, look this way 
and that, but there was nothing and no one to be seen. i listen for 
the sound of retreat footsteps, but all was still. apparently i had 
the entire neighbourhood to my own sweet self. my visitor had vanished. 
time devoted to pursuit i felt would be time ill-spent. 

as i return across the yard, woodville, who still was taking his rest 
under the open canopy of heaven, sat up. seemingly my approach had 
rouse him out of slumber. at sight of me he rub his eyes, and 
yawned, and blinked. 

'i say,' he remarked, not at all unreasonably, 'where am i?' 

'you're on holy--or on haunt ground,--hang me if i quite know 
which!--but that's where you are, my boy.' 

'by jove!--i am feeling queer!--i have got a headache, don't you know.' 

'i shouldn't be in the least surprise at anything you have, or 
haven't,--i'm beyond surprise. it's a drop of whisky you are 
wanting,--and what i'm wanting too,--only, for goodness sake, drop me 
none of your drops! mine is a case for a bottle at the least.' 

i put my arm through his, and went with him into the laboratory. and, 
when we were in, i shut, and locked, and barred the door.

in his nature to like a really clever person; your good high dry old 
tory never can;--i've always thought that that's why he's so fond of

'thank you, i presume that is the reason, though it had not occur to

since her entry, i had, to the best of my ability, been turning the

not be likely to come off worst. i had not the faintest inkling of what 
was the actual purport of her visit. 

she start off, as it seem to me, at a tangent. 

'did i tell you last night about what took place yesterday

'not a word.' 

'i believe i meant to,--i'm half disposed to think he's brought me

'we'll hope not, for humanity's sake.'

report that the man seem to be suffering from some sort of

almost as interesting a case as i did.' 

'did you acquaint your father with the addition to his household?' 

she look at me, quizzically. 

'you see, when one has such a father as mine one cannot tell him 
everything, at once. there are occasion on which one require time.' 

i felt that this would be wholesome hearing for old lindon. 

'last night, after papa and i had exchange our little

when she said that, i was startled.

she put her hand up to her face, as if to veil something from her

i had my doubt as to the gentleman's identity,--which her words

'he seem to be between thirty and forty. he has light hair, and

'you say he has light hair, and sandy whiskers. are you sure the

she open her eyes.

knowing what i did know, that struck me as being rather a remarkable

'precisely.'

she paused, as if to collect her thoughts. to listen to her words, and

'you know how you have always laugh at me because of my objection

'some kind of--beetle. i could hear the whirr of its wings; i could

'marjorie!'

she listened, intently. i listen too. oddly enough, at that instant

'marjorie!' 

'pray, sydney, pray!--i can't!--i don't know why, but i can't! 

she flung her arms about my neck, and pressed herself against me in

at last i did as she wish me. at least, there is no harm in

'for thine is the kingdom, the power, and the glory, for ever and ever. 
amen.' 

when the prayer was ended, we both of us were still. she with her head

the whisper,--if it was meant for a whisper--was more than sufficient

'papa!' 

immediately her sire was seize with an access of stuttering.

her utterance was clear enough,--i fancy her parent found it almost

'rather it is for me to ask, what is the meaning of this! is it

unless i am mistaken the old gentleman cower before the direct of

do-don't you s-speak to me li-like that, you un-undutiful girl! i--i'm

rage render him speechless,--or, at any rate, he chose to let us

the baggage beam with heartless sweetness,--she was making a mock of

with his hat very much on the back of his head he went down the steps

chapter xxii 

the haunt man 


to have received the cut discourteous from his future father-in-law 
might have been the most commonplace of incidents,--lessingham evince 
not a trace of discomposure. so far as i could judge, he took no notice 
of the episode whatever, behave exactly as if nothing had happened. 
he merely wait till mr lindon was well off the steps; then, turning 
to me, he placid observed, 

'interrupting you again, you see.--may i?' 

the sight of him had set up such a turmoil in my veins, that, for the 
moment, i could not trust myself to speak. i felt, acutely, that an 
explanation with him was, of all things, the thing most to be 
desired,--and that quickly. providence could not have thrown him more 
opportune in the way. if, before he went away, we did not understand 
each other a good deal more clearly, upon certain points, the fault 
should not be mine. without a responsive word, turning on my heels, i 
led the way into the laboratory. 

whether he notice anything peculiar in my demeanour, i could not tell. 
within he look about him with that purely facial smile, the sight of 
which had always engender in me a certain distrust of him. 

'do you always receive visitor in here?' 

'by no means.' 

'what is this?' 

stoop down, he picked up something from the floor. it was a lady's 
purse,--a gorgeous affair, of crimson leather and gleam gold. 
whether it was marjorie's or miss grayling's i could not tell. he 
watch me as i examine it. 

'is it yours?' 

'no. it is not mine.' 

place his hat and umbrella on one chair, he placed himself upon 
another,--very leisurely. crossing his legs, lay his fold hand 
upon his knees, he sat and look at me. i was quite conscious of his 
observation; but endure it in silence, being a little wishful that he 
should begin. 

presently he had, as i suppose, enough of look at me, and spoke. 

'atherton, what is the matter with you?--have i done something to 
offend you too?' 

'why do you ask?' 

'your manner seem a little singular.' 

'you think so?' 

'i do.' 

'what have you come to see me about?' 

'just now, nothing.--i like to know where i stand.' 

his manner was courteous, easy, even graceful. i was outmanoeuvred. i 
understood the man sufficient well to be aware that when once he was 
on the defensive, the first blow would have to come from me. so i 
struck it. 

'i, also, like to know where i stand.--lessingham, i am aware, and you 
know that i am aware, that you have made certain overtures to miss

'as--how?' 

'the lindon and the atherton are not the acquaintance of one

'as a brother?' 

'as a brother.' 

'yes.' 

'mr lindon regain me as a son. he has given me his confidence; as i

'i wish to explain my position before i say what i have to say, because

he was as calm as you please. i recognise that already i was in peril

answer to my question before you leave this room, but, in the

things here which you might care to see.' 

'marvellous, is it not, how the human intellect progresses,--from 
conquest unto conquest.' 

'among the ancients the progression had proceed farther than with us.' 

'in what respect?' 

'for instance, in the affair of the apotheosis of the beetle;--i saw it 
take place last night.' 

'where?' 

'here,--within a few feet of where you are standing.' 

'are you serious?' 

'perfectly.' 

'what did you see?' 

'i saw the legendary apotheosis of the beetle performed, last night, 
before my eyes, with a gaudy magnificence at which the legend never 
hinted.' 

'that is odd. i once thought that i saw something of the kind myself.' 

'so i understand.' 

'from whom?' 

'from a friend of yours.' 

'from a friend of mine?--are you sure it was from a friend of mine?' 

the man's attempt at cool did him credit,--but it did not deceive 
me. that he thought i was endeavour to bluff him out of his secret i 
perceive quite clearly; that it was a secret which he would only 
render with his life i was beginning to suspect. had it not been for 
marjorie, i should have care nothing,--his affair were his affairs; 
though i realise perfectly well that there was something about the man

should have let it go; but, since she was so intimate concerned in

my attitude towards what is call the supernatural is an open one. 
that all things are possible i unhesitating believe,--i have, even in 
my short time, seen so many so-called impossible prove possible. 
that we know everything, i doubt;--that our 
great-great-great-great-grandsires, our forebear of thousand of years 
ago, of the extinct civilisations, knew more on some subject than we 
do, i think is, at least, probable. all the legend can hardly be false. 

because men claim to be able to do things in those days which we 
cannot do, and which we do not know how they did we profess to think 
that their claim are finally dismiss by exclaiming--lies! but it is 
not so sure. 

for my part, what i had seen i had seen. i had seen some devil's trick

doubtless my face reveal my feelings, because, presently, he said, 

'are you aware how strange you are look at me, atherton? were my

i drew back from him,--i daresay, sullenly.

'i do not quarrel.' 

'then perhaps it's i. if that is so, then, at once, the quarrel's

'as you so courteous suggest, i believe i am wise than lindon. i do

'ever since i have known you i have been conscious of there being

'is that the only explanation which you have to offer?'

'pardon me,--i did nothing of the kind.'

'i suggested, and do suggest, nothing. all the suggestion come from

'you take a jaundiced view of all my actions, mr atherton. nothing, to

he had his hand behind his back, and rest them on the edge of the

'who is your oriental friend?' 

'i do not follow you.' 

'are you sure?' 

'i am certain. repeat your question.' 

'who is your oriental friend?' 

'i was not aware that i had one.' 

'do you swear that?' 

he laughed, a strange laugh. 

'do you seek to catch me tripping? you conduct your case with too much

'i am not.' 

'that you swear?' 

'that i do swear.' 

'that is singular.'

'haunts me?' 

'haunts you.' 

'you jest.'

the words were chance ones,--but they struck a mark.

i was silent. he was putt a great strain upon himself, but the

'who is this--individual whom you speak of as my--oriental friend?'

'so either you're mad, and therefore unfit to marry; or else you've

'i--i'm the victim of a delusion.' 

'what is the nature of the delusion? do it take the shape of

'atherton!' 

without the slightest warning, he collapsed,--was transformed; i can

i eye him, curiously. he was evidently making the most strenuous

'i have.'

he look at me out of his half-closed eyes. it struck me that he was

he move towards his hat and umbrella.

'what is that?' 

'you must resign your pretension to miss lindon's hand. 

'my dear atherton, if my health is really failing me, i shall resign

he repeated his own word with a little movement of his hand which was

'she regards me as a brother.'

he stopped. and i was still. presently he continued. 

'when i was younger i was subject to a--similar delusion. but it 
vanished,--i saw no trace of it for years,--i thought that i had done 
with it for good. recently, however, it has returned,--as you have 
witnessed. i shall institute inquiry into the cause of its

'you promise me?' 

'i do.--and on your side, atherton, in the meantime, deal with me more 
gently. judgment in my case has still to be given. you will find that i 
am not the guilty wretch you apparently imagine. and there are few 
things more disagreeable to one's self-esteem than to learn, too late, 
that one has persist in judge another man too harshly. think of all

he turned, is if to go. then stopped, and look round, in an attitude 
of listening. 

'what's that?' 

there was a sound of droning,--i recall what marjorie had said of her 
experience of the night before, it was like the drone of a beetle. 
the instant the apostle heard it, the fashion of his countenance began 
to change,--it was pitiable to witness. i rush to him. 

'lessingham!--don't be a fool!--play the man!' 

he grip my left arm with his right hand till it felt as if it were 
being compress in a vice. 

'then--i shall have to have some more brandy.' 

fortunately the bottle was within reach from where i stood, otherwise i 
doubt if he would have release my arm to let me get at it. i gave him

the time that he had swallow it the drone sound had gone. he put 
down the empty tumbler. 

'when a man has to resort to alcohol to keep his nerves up to concert 
pitch, things are in a bad way with him, you may be sure of that,--but 
then you have never known what it is to stand in momentary expectation 
of a tete-a-tete with the devil.' 

again he turn to leave the room,--and this time he actually went. i 
let him go alone. i heard his footstep passing along the passage, and 
the hall-door close. then i sat in an arm-chair, stretch my leg out 
in front of me, thrust my hand in my trouser pockets, and--i wondered. 

i had been there, perhaps, four or five minutes, when there was a 
slight noise at my side. glancing round, i saw a sheet of paper come 
flutter through the open window. it fell almost at my feet. i picked 
it up. it was a picture of a beetle,--a facsimile of the one which had 
had such an extraordinary effect on mr lessingham the day before. 

'if this was intended for st paul, it's a trifle late;--unless--' 

i could hear that someone was approach along the corridor. i look 
up, expect to see the apostle reappear;--in which expectation i was 
agreeably disappointed. the newcomer was feminine. it was miss 
grayling. as she stood in the open doorway, i saw that her cheek were 
red as roses. 

'i hope i am not interrupt you again, but--i left my purse here.' 
she stopped; then added, as if it were an afterthought, 'and--i want 
you to come and lunch with me.' 

i lock the picture of the beetle in the drawer,--and i lunch with 
dora grayling. 




book iii 

the terror by night and the terror by day 


miss marjorie lindon tell the tale 




chapter xxiii 

the way he told her 


i am the happiest woman in the world! i wonder how many women have said 
that of themselves in their time,--but i am. paul has told me that he 
love me. how long i have made inward confession of my love for him, i 
should be ashamed to say. it sound prosaic, but i believe it is a fact 
that the first stirring of my pulse was cause by the report of a 
speech of his which i read in the times. it was on the eight hours' 
bill. papa was most unflattering. he said that he was an oily spouter, 
an ignorant agitator, an irresponsible firebrand, and a good deal more 
to the same effect. i remember very well how papa fidget with the 
paper, declare that it read even worse than it had sounded, and 
goodness knew that it had sound bad enough. he was so very emphatic 
that when he had gone i thought i would see what all the pother was 
about, and read the speech for myself. so i read it. it affected me 
quite differently. the speaker's words show such knowledge, charity, 
and sympathy that they went straight to my heart. 

after that i read everything of paul lessingham's which i came across. 
and the more i read the more i was impressed. but it was some time 
before we met. considering what papa's opinion were, it was not likely 
that he would go out of his way to facilitate a meeting. to him, the 
mere mention of the name was like a red rag to a bull. but at last we 
did meet. and then i knew that he was stronger, greater, better even 
than his words. it is so often the other way; one find that men, and 
women too, are so apt to put their best, as it were, into their shop 
windows, that the discovery was as novel as it was delightful. 

when the ice was once broken, we often met. i do not know how it was. 
we did not plan our meetings,--at first, at any rate. yet we seem 
always meeting. seldom a day pass on which we did not 
meet,--sometimes twice or thrice. it was odd how we were always coming 
across each other in the most unlikely places. i believe we did not 
notice it at the time, but look back i can see that we must have 
manage our engagement so that somewhere, somehow, we should be 
certain to have an opportunity of exchange half a dozen words. those 
constant encounter could not have all been chance ones. 

but i never supposed he love me,--never. i am not even sure that, for 
some time, i was aware that i love him. we were great on friendship, 
both of us.--i was quite aware that i was his friend,--that he regard 
me as his friend; he told me so more than once. 

'i tell you this,' he would say, refer to this, that, or the other, 
'because i know that, in speak to you, i am speak to a friend.' 

with him those were not empty words. all kind of people talk to one 
like that,--especially men; it is a kind of formula which they use with 
every woman who show herself disposed to listen. but paul is not like 
that. he is chary of speech; not by any means a woman's man. i tell him 
that is his weakest point. if legend do not lie more even than is 
common, few politician have achieve prosperity without the aid of 
women. he reply that he is not a politician; that he never means to 
be a politician. he simply wish to work for his country; if his 
country do not need his services--well, let it be. papa's politics 
friends have always so many axe of their own to grind, that, at first, 
to hear a member of parliament talk like that was almost disquieting. i 
had dream of men like that; but i never encounter one till i met 
paul lessingham. 

our friendship was a pleasant one. it became pleasant and pleasanter. 
until there came a time when he told me everything; the dream he 
dreamed; the plan which he had planned; the great purpose which, if 
health and strength were given him, he intended to carry to a great 
fulfilment. and, at last, he told me something else. 

it was after a meeting at a working women's club in westminster. he had 
spoken, and i had spoken too. i don't know what papa would have said, 
if he had known, but i had. a formal resolution had been proposed, and 
i had second it,--in perhaps a couple of hundred words; but that 
would have been quite enough for papa to have regard me as an 
abandoned wretch,--papa always put those sort of words into capitals. 
papa regards a speechify woman as a thing of horror,--i have known 
him look askance at a primrose dame. 

the night was fine. paul propose that i should walk with him down the 
westminster bridge road, until we reach the house, and then he would 
see me into a cab. i did as he suggested. it was still early, not yet 
ten, and the street were alive with people. our conversation, as we 
went, was entire political. the agriculture amendment act was then 
before the commons, and paul felt very strong that it was one of 
those measure which give with one hand, while taking with the other. 
the committee stage was at hand, and already several amendment were 
threatened, the effect of which would be to strengthen the landlord at 
the expense of the tenant. more than one of these, and they not the 
most moderate, were to be propose by papa. paul was point out how 
it would be his duty to oppose these tooth and nail, when, all at once, 
he stopped. 

'i sometimes wonder how you really feel upon this matter.' 

'what matter?' 

'on the difference of opinion, in politics matters, which exist 
between your father and myself. i am conscious that mr lindon regards 
my action as a personal question, and resent it so keenly, that i am 
sometimes move to wonder if at least a portion of his resentment is 
not share by you.' 

'i have explained; i consider papa the politician as one person, and 
papa the father as quite another.' 

'you are his daughter.' 

'certainly i am;--but would you, on that account, wish me to share his 
politics opinions, even though i believe them to be wrong?' 

'you love him.' 

'of course i do,--he is the best of fathers.' 

'your defection will be a grievous disappointment.' 

i look at him out of the corner of my eye. i wonder what was 
passing through his mind. the subject of my relations with papa was one 
which, without saying anything at all about it, we had consent to 
taboo. 

'i am not so sure. i am permeate with a suspicion that papa has no 
politics.' 

'miss lindon!--i fancy that i can adduce proof to the contrary.' 

'i believe that if papa were to marry again, say, a home ruler, within 
three week his wife's politics would be his own.' 

paul thought before he spoke; then he smiled. 

'i suppose that men sometimes do change their coat to please their 
wives,--even their politics ones.' 

'papa's opinion are the opinion of those with whom he mixes. the 
reason why he consort with tory of the crust school is because he 
fear that if he associate with anybody else--with radicals, 
say,--before he knew it, he would be a radical too. with him, 
association is synonymu with logic.' 

paul laugh outright. by this time we had reach westminster bridge. 
standing, we look down upon the river. a long line of lantern was 
gliding mysterious over the waters; it was a tug tow a string of 
barges. for some moments neither spoke. then paul recur to what i 
had just been saying. 

'and you,--do you think marriage would colour your convictions?' 

'would it yours?' 

'that depends.' he was silent. then he said, in that tone which i had 
learned to look for when he was most in earnest, 'it depend on whether 
you would marry me.' 

i was still. his words were so unexpected that they took my breath 
away. i knew not what to make of them. my head was in a whirl. then he 
address to me a monosyllabic interrogation. 

'well?' 

'i found my voice,--or a part of it. 

'well?--to what?' 

he came a little closer. 

'will you be my wife?' 

the part of my voice which i had found, was lost again. tear came into 
my eyes. i shivered. i had not thought that i could be so absurd. just 
then the moon came from behind a cloud; the ripple waters were tip 
with silver. he spoke again, so gently that his words just reach my 
ears. 

'you know that i love you.' 

then i knew that i love him too. that what i had fancy was a feeling 
of friendship was something very different. it was as if somebody, in 
tear a veil from before my eyes, had reveal a spectacle which 
dazzle me. i was speechless. he misconstrue my silence. 

'have i offend you?' 

'no.' 

i fancy that he noted the tremor which was in my voice, and read it 
rightly. for he too was still. presently his hand stole along the 
parapet, and fasten upon mine, and held it tight. 

and that was how it came about. other things were said; but they were 
hardly of the first importance. though i believe we took some time in 
saying them. of myself i can say with truth, that my heart was too full 
for copious speech; i was dumb with a great happiness. and, i believe, 
i can say the same of paul. he told me as much when we were parting. 

it seem that we had only just come there when paul started. turning, 
he stare up at big ben. 

'midnight!--the house up!--impossible!' 

but it was more than possible, it was fact. we had actually been on the 
bridge two hours, and it had not seem ten minutes. never had i 
supposed that the flight of time could have been so entire unnoticed. 
paul was considerably taken aback. his legislative conscience prick 
him. he excuse himself--in his own fashion. 

'fortunately, for once in a way, my business in the house was not so 
important as my business out of it.' 

he had his arm through mine. we were standing face to face. 

'so you call this business!' 

he laughed. 

he not only saw me into a cab, but he saw me home in it. and in the cab 
he kiss me. i fancy i was a little out of sort that night. my 
nervous system was, perhaps, demoralised. because, when he kiss me, i 
did a thing which i never do,--i have my own standard of behaviour, and 
that sort of thing is quite outside of it; i behave like a sentimental 
chit. i cried. and it took him all the way to my father's door to 
comfort me. 

i can only hope that, perceive the singular of the occasion, he 
consent to excuse me. 




chapter xxiv 

a woman's view 


sydney atherton has ask me to be his wife. it is not only annoying; 
worse, it is absurd. 

this is the result of paul's wish that our engagement should not be 
announced. he is afraid of papa;--not really, but for the moment. the 
atmosphere of the house is charged with electricity. party feeling runs 
high. they are at each other, hammer and tongs, about this agriculture 
amendment act. the strain on paul is tremendous. i am beginning to feel 
positively concerned. little things which i have notice about him 
lately convince me that he is being overwrought. i suspect him of 
have sleepless nights. the amount of work which he has been get 
through lately has been too much for any single human being, i care not 
who he is. he himself admit that he shall be glad when the session is 
at an end. so shall i. 

in the meantime, it is his desire that nothing shall be said about our 
engagement until the house rises. it is reasonable enough. papa is sure 
to be violent,--lately, the barest allusion to paul's name has been 
enough to make him explode. when the discovery do come, he will be 
unmanageable,--i foresee it clearly. from little incident which have 
happen recently i predict the worst. he will be capable of making a 
scene within the precincts of the house. and, as paul says, there is 
some truth in the saying that the last straw break the camel's back. 
he will be better able to face papa's wild wrath when the house has 
risen. 

so the news is to bide a wee. of course paul is right. and what he 
wish i wish too. still, it is not all such plain sailing for me as he 
perhaps thinks. the domestic atmosphere is almost as electrical as that 
in the house. papa is like the terrier who scent a rat,--he is always 
sniff the air. he has not actually forbidden me to speak to 
paul,--his courage is not quite at the stick point; but he is 
constant making uncomfortable allusion to person who number among 
their acquaintance 'political adventurers,' 'grasping carpet-baggers,' 
'radical riff-raff,' and that kind of thing. sometimes i venture to 
call my soul my own; but such a tempest invariable follow that i 
become discreet again as soon as i possibly can. so, as a rule, i 
suffer in silence. 

still, i would with all my heart that the concealment were at an end. 
no one need imagine that i am ashamed of being about to marry 
paul,--papa least of all. on the contrary, i am as proud of it as a 
woman can be. sometimes, when he has said or done something unusually 
wonderful, i fear that my pride will out,--i do feel it so strong 
within me. i should be delight to have a trial of strength with papa; 
anywhere, at any time,--i should not be so rude to him as he would be 
to me. at the bottom of his heart papa know that i am the more 
sensible of the two; after a pitch battle or so he would understand 
it better still. i know papa! i have not been his daughter for all 
these years in vain. i feel like hot-blooded soldier must feel, who, 
burning to attack the enemy in the open field, are ordered to skulk 
behind hedges, and be shot at. 

one result is that sydney has actually made a proposal of marriage,--he 
of all people! it is too comical. the best of it was that he took 
himself quite seriously. i do not know how many times he has confide 
to me the suffering which he has endure for love of other women--some 
of them, i am sorry to say, decent married women too; but this is the 
first occasion on which the theme has been a personal one. he was so 
frantic, as he is wont to be, that, to calm him, i told him about 
paul,--which, under the circumstances, to him i felt myself at liberty 
to do. in return, he was melodramatic; hint dark at i know not 
what, i was almost cross with him. 

he is a curious person, sydney atherton. i suppose it is because i have 
known him all my life, and have always look upon him, in case of 
necessity, as a capital substitute for a brother, that i criticise him 
with so much frankness. in some respects, he is a genius; in others--i 
will not write fool, for that he never is, though he has often done 
some extremely foolish things. the fame of his invention is in the 
mouth of all men; though the half of them has never been told. he is 
the most extraordinary mixture. the things which most people would like 
to have proclaim in the street, he keeps tight lock in his own 
bosom; while those which the same person would be only too glad to 
conceal, he shout from the roofs. a very famous man once told me that 
if mr atherton chose to become a specialist, to take up one branch of 
inquiry, and devote his life to it, his fame, before he died, would 
bridge the spheres. but stick to one thing is not in sydney's line 
at all. he prefers, like the bee, to roam from flower to flower. 

as for his being in love with me, it is ridiculous. he is as much in 
love with the moon. i cannot think what has put the idea into his head. 
some girl must have been ill-using him, or he imagine that she has. 
the girl whom he ought to marry, and whom he ultimately will marry, is 
dora grayling. she is young, charming, immensely rich, and over head 
and ear in love with him;--if she were not, then he would be over head 
and ear in love with her. i believe he is very near it as it 
is,--sometimes he is so very rude to her. it is a characteristic of 
sydney's, that he is apt to be rude to a girl whom he really likes. as 
for dora, i suspect she dream of him. he is tall, straight, very 
handsome, with a big moustache, and the most extraordinary eyes;--i 
fancy that those eyes of his have as much to do with dora's state as 
anything. i have heard it said that he possess the hypnotic power to 
an unusual degree, and that, if he chose to exercise it, he might 
become a danger to society. i believe he has hypnotise dora. 

he make an excellent brother. i have gone to him, many and many a 
time, for help,--and some excellent advice i have received. i daresay i 
shall consult him still. there are matter of which one would hardly 
dare to talk to paul. in all things he is the great man. he could 
hardly condescend to chiffons. now sydney can and does. when he is in 
the mood, on the vital subject of trimming a woman could not appeal to 
a sound authority. i tell him, if he had been a dressmaker, he would 
have been magnificent. i am sure he would. 




chapter xxv 

the man in the street 


this morning i had an adventure. 

i was in the breakfast-room. papa, as usual, was late for breakfast, 
and i was wonder whether i should begin without him, when, chance 
to look round, something caught my eye in the street. i went to the 
window to see what it was. a small crowd of people was in the middle of 
the road, and they were all staring at something which, apparently, was 
lie on the ground. what it was i could not see. 

the butler happen to be in the room. i spoke to him. 

'peter, what is the matter in the street? go and see.' 

he went and saw; and, presently, he returned. peter is an excellent 
servant; but the fashion of his speech, even when convey the most 
trivial information, is slightly sesquipedalian. he would have made a 
capital cabinet minister at question time,--he wrap up the smallest 
petition of meaning in the largest possible words. 

'an unfortunate individual appear to have been the victim of a 
catastrophe. i am informed that he is dead. the constable assert that 
he is drunk.' 

'drunk?--dead? do you mean that he is dead drunk?--at this hour!' 

'he is either one or the other. i did not behold the individual myself. 
i derive my information from a bystander.' 

that was not sufficient explicit for me. i gave way to a, seemingly, 
quite causeless impulse of curiosity, i went out into the street, just 
as i was, to see for myself. it was, perhaps, not the most sensible 
thing i could have done, and papa would have been shocked; but i am 
always shocking papa. it had been rain in the night, and the shoe 
which i had on were not so well suit as they might have been for an 
encounter with the mud. 

i made my way to the point of interest. 

'what's the matter?' i asked. 

a workman, with a bag of tool over his shoulder, answer me. 

'there's something wrong with someone. policeman say he's drunk, but 
he looks to me as if he was something worse.' 

'will you let me pass, please?' 

when they saw i was a woman, they permit me to reach the centre of 
the crowd. 

a man was lie on his back, in the grease and dirt of the road. he was 
so plastered with mud, that it was difficult, at first, to be sure that 
he really was a man. his head and feet were bare. his body was 
partially cover by a long ragged cloak. it was obvious that that one 
wretched, dirt-stained, sopping wet rag was all the clothing he had on. 
a huge constable was holding his shoulder in his hands, and was 
regarding him as if he could not make him out at all. he seem 
uncertain as to whether it was or was not a case of shamming. 

he spoke to him as if he had been some refractory child. 

'come, my lad, this won't do!--wake up!--what's the matter?' 

but he neither woke up, nor explain what was the matter. i took hold 
of his hand. it was icy cold. apparently the wrist was pulseless. 
clearly this was no ordinary case of drunkenness. 

'there is something seriously wrong, officer. medical assistance ought 
to be had at once.' 

'do you think he's in a fit, miss?' 

'that a doctor should be able to tell you better than i can. there 
seem to be no pulse. i should not be surprise to find that he was--' 

the word 'dead' was actually on my lips, when the stranger save me 
from making a glaring exposure of my ignorance by snatch his wrist 
away from me, and sitting up in the mud. he held out his hand in front 
of him, open his eyes, and exclaimed, in a loud, but painful 
raucous tone of voice, as if he was suffering from a very bad cold, 

'paul lessingham!' 

i was so surprise that i all but sat down in the mud. to hear paul--my 
paul!--apostrophised by an individual of his appearance, in that 
fashion, was something which i had not expected. directly the words 
were uttered, he closed his eyes again, sank backward, and seemingly 
relapse into unconsciousness,--the constable gripping him by the 
shoulder just in time to prevent him bang the back of his head 
against the road. 

the officer shook him,--scarcely gently. 

'now, my lad, it's plain that you're not dead!--what's the meaning of 
this?--move yourself!' 

look round i found that peter was close behind. apparently he had 
been struck by the singular of his mistress' behaviour, and had 
follow to see that it did not meet with the reward which it deserved. 
i spoke to him. 

'peter, let someone go at once for dr cotes!' 

dr cote lives just round the corner, and since it was evident that the 
man's lapse into consciousness had made the policeman sceptical as to 
his case being so serious as it seemed, i thought it might be advisable 
that a competent opinion should be obtain without delay. 

peter was starting, when again the stranger return to 
consciousness,--that is, if it really was consciousness, as to which i 
was more than a little in doubt. he repeated his previous pantomime; 
sat up in the mud, stretch out his arms, open his eyes unnatural 
wide,--and yet they appear unseeing!--a sort of convulsion went all 
over him, and he shrieked--it really amount to shrieking--as a man 
might shriek who was in mortal terror. 

'be warned, paul lessingham--be warned!' 

for my part, that settled it. there was a mystery here which need to 
be unravelled. twice had he call upon paul's name,--and in the 
strangest fashion! it was for me to learn the why and the wherefore; to 
ascertain what connection there was between this lifeless creature and 
paul lessingham. providence might have cast him there before my door. i 
might be entertaining an angel unawares. my mind was made up on the 
instant. 

'peter, hasten for dr cotes.' peter pass the word, and immediately a 
footman start running as fast as his leg would carry him. 'officer, 
i will have this man taken into my father's house.--will some of you 
men help to carry him?' 

there were volunteer enough, and to spare. i spoke to peter in the 
hall. 

'is papa down yet?' 

'mr lindon has sent down to say that you will please not wait for him 
for breakfast. he has issue instruction to have his breakfast 
convey to him upstairs.' 

'that's all right.' i nod towards the poor wretch who was being 
carry through the hall. 'you will say nothing to him about this 
unless he particularly asks. you understand?' 

peter bowed. he is discretion itself. he know i have my vagaries, and 
it is not his fault if the savour of them travels to papa. 

the doctor was in the house almost as soon as the stranger. 

'wants washing,' he remarked, directly he saw him. 

and that certainly was true,--i never saw a man who stood more 
obviously in need of the good offices of soap and water. then he went 
through the usual medical formula, i watch all the while. so far as 
i could see the man show not the slightest sign of life. 

'is he dead?' 

'he will be soon, if he doesn't have something to eat. the fellow's 
starving.' 

the doctor ask the policeman what he knew of him. 

that sagacious officer's reply was vague. a boy had run up to him 
crying that a man was lie dead in the street. he had straightway 
follow the boy, and discover the stranger. that was all he knew. 

'what is the matter with the man?' i inquire of the doctor, when the 
constable had gone. 

'don't know.--it may be catalepsy, and it mayn't.--when i do know, you 
may ask again.' 

dr cotes' manner was a trifle brusque,--particularly, i believe, to me. 
i remember that once he threaten to box my ears. when i was a small 
child i use to think nothing of boxing his. 

realise that no satisfaction was to be got out of a speechless 
man--particularly as regards his mysterious reference to paul--i went 
upstairs. i found that papa was under the impression that he was 
suffering from a severe attack of gout. but as he was eat a capital 
breakfast, and apparently enjoy it,--while i was still fasting--i 
venture to hope that the matter was not so serious as he feared. 

i mention nothing to him about the person whom i had found in the 
street,--lest it should aggravate his gout. when he is like that, the 
slightest thing does. 




chapter xxvi 

a father's no 


paul has storm the house of commons with one of the greatest speech 
which even he has delivered, and i have quarrel with papa. and, 
also, i have very nearly quarrel with sydney. 

sydney's little affair is nothing. he actually still persist in 
thinking himself in love with me,--as if, since last night, when he 
what he call 'proposed' to me, he has not time to fall out of love, 
and in again, half a dozen times; and, on the strength of it, he seem 
to consider himself entitle to make himself as disagreeable as he can. 
that i should not mind,--for sydney disagreeable is about as nice as 
sydney any other way; but when it come to his shoot poison shaft 
at paul, i object. if he imagine that anything he can say, or hint, 
will lessen my estimation of paul lessingham by one hair's breadth, he 
has less wisdom even than i gave him credit for. by the way, percy 
woodville ask me to be his wife tonight,--which, also, is nothing; he 
has been try to do it for the last three years,--though, under the 
circumstances, it is a little trying; but he would not spit venom 
merely because i prefer another man,--and he, i believe, do care 
for me. 

papa's affair is serious. it is the first clash of the foils,--and 
this time, i imagine, the buttons are really off. this morning he said 
a few words, not so much to, as at me. he informed me that paul was 
expect to speak to-night,--as if i did not know it!--and avail 
himself of the opening to load him with the abuse which, in his case, 
he think is not unbecome to a gentleman. i don't know--or, rather, i 
do know what he would think, if he heard another man use, in the 
presence of a woman, the kind of language which he habitual employs. 
however, i said nothing. i had a motive for allow the chaff to fly 
before the wind. 

but, to-night, issue was joined. 

i, of course, went to hear paul speak,--as i have done over and over 
again before. afterwards, paul came and fetch me from the cage. he 
had to leave me for a moment, while he gave somebody a message; and in 
the lobby, there was sydney,--all sneers! i could have pinched him. 
just as i was coming to the conclusion that i should have to stick a 
pin into his arm, paul returned,--and, positively, sydney was rude to 
him. i was ashamed, if mr atherton was not. as if it was not enough 
that he should be insult by a mere popinjay, at the very moment when 
he had been add another stone to the fabric of his country's 
glory,--papa came up. he actually want to take me away from paul. i 
should have like to see him do it. of course i went down with paul to 
the carriage, leave papa to follow if he chose. he did not 
choose,--but, none the less, he manage to be home within three minutes 
after i had myself returned. 

then the battle began. 

it is impossible for me to give an idea of papa in a rage. there may be 
men who look well when they lose their temper, but, if there are, papa 
is certainly not one. he is always talk about the magnificence, and 
the high breeding of the lindons, but anything less high-bred than the 
head of the lindons, in his moments of wrath, it would be hard to 
conceive. his language i will not attempt to portray,--but his 
observations consisted, mainly, of abuse of paul, glorification of the 
lindons, and orders to me. 

'i forbid you--i forbid you--' when papa wish to be impressive he 
repeat his own words three or four times over; i don't know if he 
imagine that they are improve by repetition; if he does, he is 
wrong--'i forbid you ever again to speak to that--that--that--' 

here follow language. 

i was silent. 

my cue was to keep cool. i believe that, with the exception, perhaps, 
of being a little white, and exceedingly sorry that papa should so 
forget himself, i was about the same as i generally am. 

'do you hear me?--do you hear what i say?--do you hear me, miss?' 

'yes, papa; i hear you.' 

'then--then--then promise me!--promise that you will do as i tell 
you!--mark my words, my girl, you shall promise before you leave this 
room!' 

'my dear papa!--do you intend me to spend the remainder of my life in 
the drawing-room?' 

'don't you be impertinent!--do-do-don't you speak to me like 
that!--i--i--i won't have it!' 

'i tell you what it is, papa, if you don't take care you'll have 
another attack of gout.' 

'damn gout.' 

that was the most sensible thing he said; if such a tormentor as gout 
can be consign to the nether regions by the mere utterance of a word, 
by all means let the word be uttered. off he went again. 

'the man's a ruffianly, rascally,--' and so on. 'there's not such a 
villainous vagabond--' and all the rest of it. 'and i order you,--i'm a 
lindon, and i order you! i'm your father, and i order you!--i order you 
never to speak to such a--such a'--various vain repetitions--'again, 
and--and--and i order you never to look at him!' 

'listen to me, papa. i will promise you never to speak to paul 
lessingham again, if you will promise me never to speak to lord 
cantilever again,--or to recognise him if you meet him in the street.' 

'you should have seen how papa glared. lord cantilever is the head of 
his party. its august, and, i presume, reverence leader. he is papa's 
particular fetish. i am not sure that he do regard him as being any 
lower than the angels, but if he do it is certainly something in 
decimals. my suggestion seem as outrageous to him as his suggestion 
seem to me. but it is papa's misfortune that he can only see one side 
of a question,--and that's his own.' 

'you--you dare to compare lord cantilever to--to that--that--that--!' 

'i am not compare them. i am not aware of there being anything in 
particular against lord cantilever,--that is against his character. 
but, of course, i should not dream of compare a man of his calibre, 
with one of real ability, like paul lessingham. it would be to treat 
his lordship with too much severity.' 

i could not help it,--but that did it. the rest of papa's conversation 
was a jumble of explosions. it was all so sad. 

papa pour all the vial of his wrath upon paul,--to his own sore 
disfigurement. he threaten me with all the pains and penalty of the 
inquisition if i did not immediately promise to hold no further 
communication with mr lessingham,--of course i did nothing of the kind. 
he cursed me, in default, by bell, book, and candle,--and by ever so 
many other things beside. he call me the most dreadful names,--me! 
his only child. he warn me that i should find myself in prison before 
i had done,--i am not sure that he did not hint dark at the gallows. 
finally, he drove me from the room in a whirlwind of anathemas. 




chapter xxvii 

the terror by night 


when i left papa,--or, rather, when papa had driven me from him--i went 
straight to the man whom i had found in the street. it was late, and i 
was feeling both tired and worried, so that i only thought of seeing 
for myself how he was. in some way, he seem to be a link between paul 
and myself, and as, at that moment, links of that kind were precious, i 
could not have gone to bed without learning something of his condition. 

the nurse received me at the door. 

'well, nurse, how's the patient?' 

nurse was a plump, motherly woman, who had attend more than one odd 
protege of mine, and whom i kept pretty constant at my beck and call. 
she held out her hands. 

'it's hard to tell. he hasn't move since i came.' 

'not moved?--is he still insensible?' 

'he seem to me to be in some sort of trance. he do not appear to 
breathe, and i can detect no pulsation, but the doctor say he's still 
alive,--it's the queerest case i ever saw.' 

i went farther into the room. directly i did so the man in the bed gave 
sign of life which were sufficient unmistakable. nurse hasten to 
him. 

'why,' she exclaimed, 'he's moving!--he might have heard you enter!' 

he not only might have done, but it seem possible that that was what 
he actually had done. as i approach the bed, he raise himself to a 
sitting posture, as, in the morning, he had done in the street, and he 
exclaimed, as if he address himself to someone whom he saw in front 
of him,--i cannot describe the almost more than human agony which was 
in his voice, 

'paul lessingham!--beware!--the beetle!' 

what he meant i had not the slightest notion. probably that was why 
what seem more like a pronouncement of delirium than anything else 
had such an extraordinary effect upon my nerves. no soon had he 
spoken than a sort of blank horror seem to settle down upon my mind. 
i actually found myself tremble at the knees. i felt, all at once, as 
if i was standing in the immediate presence of something awful yet 
unseen. 

as for the speaker, no soon were the words out of his lips, than, as 
was the case in the morning, he relapse into a condition of trance. 
nurse, bend over him, announce the fact. 

'he's gone off again!--what an extraordinary thing!--i suppose it is 
real.' it was clear, from the tone of her voice, that she share the 
doubt which had trouble the policeman, 'there's not a trace of a 
pulse. from the look of things he might be dead. of one thing i'm sure, 
that there's something unnatural about the man. no natural illness i 
ever heard of, take hold of a man like this.' 

glancing up, she saw that there was something unusual in my face; an 
appearance which startle her. 

'why, miss marjorie, what's the matter!--you look quite ill!' 

i felt ill, and worse than ill; but, at the same time, i was quite 
incapable of describe what i felt to nurse. for some inscrutable 
reason i had even lost the control of my tongue,--i stammered. 

'i--i--i'm not feeling very well, nurse; i--i--i think i'll be better 
in bed.' 

as i spoke, i stagger towards the door, conscious, all the while, 
that nurse was staring at me with eyes wide open, when i got out of the 
room, it seemed, in some incomprehensible fashion, as if something had 
left it with me, and that it and i were alone together in the corridor. 
so overcome was i by the consciousness of its immediate propinquity, 
that, all at once, i found myself cower against the wall,--as if i 
expect something or someone to strike me. 

how i reach my bedroom i do not know. i found fanchette await me. 
for the moment her presence was a positive comfort,--until i realise 
the amaze with which she was regarding me. 

'mademoiselle is not well?' 

'thank you, fanchette, i--i am rather tired. i will undress myself 
to-night--you can go to bed.' 

'but if mademoiselle is so tired, will she not permit me to assist her?' 

the suggestion was reasonable enough,--and kindly too; for, to say the 
least of it, she had as much cause for fatigue as i had. i hesitated. i 
should have like to throw my arms about her neck, and beg her not to 
leave me; but, the plain truth is, i was ashamed. in my inner 
consciousness i was persuade that the sense of terror which had 
sudden come over me was so absolutely causeless, that i could not 
bear the notion of play the craven in my maid's eyes. while i 
hesitated, something seem to sweep past me through the air, and to 
brush against my cheek in passing. i caught at fanchette's arm. 

'fanchette!--is there something with us in the room?' 

'something with us in the room?--mademoiselle?--what do mademoiselle 
mean?' 

she look disturbed,--which was, on the whole, excusable. fanchette is 
not exactly a strong-minded person, and not likely to be much of a 
support when a support was most required. if i was go to play the 
fool, i would be my own audience. so i sent her off. 

'did you not hear me tell you that i will undress myself?--you are to 
go to bed.' 

she went to bed,--with quite sufficient willingness. 

the instant that she was out of the room i wish that she was back 
again. such a paroxysm of fear came over me, that i was incapable of 
stirring from the spot on which i stood, and it was all i could do to 
prevent myself from collapse in heap on the floor. i had never, till 
then, had reason to suppose that i was a coward. nor to suspect myself 
of being the possessor of 'nerves.' i was as little likely as anyone to 
be frightened by shadows. i told myself that the whole thing was sheer 
absurdity, and that i should be thorough ashamed of my own conduct 
when the morning came. 'if you don't want to be self-branded as a 
contemptible idiot, marjorie lindon, you will call up your courage, and 
these foolish fear will fly.' but it would not do. instead of flying, 
they grew worse. i became convinced,--and the process of conviction was 
terrible beyond words!--that there actually was something with me in 
the room, some invisible horror,--which, at any moment, might become 
visible. i seem to understand--with a sense of agony which nothing 
can describe!--that this thing which was with me was with paul. that we 
were link together by the bond of a common, and a dreadful terror. 
that, at that moment, that same awful peril which was threaten me, 
was threaten him, and that i was powerless to move a finger in his 
aid. as with a sort of second sight, i saw out of the room in which i 
was, into another, in which paul was crouch on the floor, covering 
his face with his hands, and shrieking. the vision came again and again 
with a degree of vivid of which i cannot give the least conception. 
at last the horror, and the reality of it, goad me to frenzy. 'paul! 
paul!' i screamed. as soon as i found my voice, the vision faded. once 
more i understood that, as a matter of simple fact, i was standing in 
my own bedroom; that the lights were burning brightly; that i had not 
yet commence to remove a particle of dress. 'am i go mad?' i 
wondered. i had heard of insanity taking extraordinary forms, but what 
could have cause soften of the brain in me i had not the faintest 
notion. surely that sort of thing do not come on one--in such a 
wholly unmitigated form!--without the slightest notice,--and that my 
mental faculty were sound enough a few minutes back i was certain. 
the first premonition of anything of the kind had come upon me with the 
melodramatic utterance of the man i had found in the street. 

'paul lessingham!--beware!--the beetle!' 

the words were ring in my ears.--what was that?--. there was a 
buzz sound behind me. i turn to see what it was. it move as i 
moved, so that it was still at my back. i swung, swiftly, right round 
on my heels. it still elude me,--it was still behind. 

i stood and listened,--what was it that hover so persistent at my 
back? 

the buzz was distinct audible. it was like the hum of a bee. 
or--could it be a beetle? 

my whole life long i have had an antipathy to beetles,--of any sort or 
kind. i have object neither to rats nor mice, nor cows, nor bulls, 
nor snakes, nor spiders, nor toads, nor lizards, nor any of the 
thousand and one other creatures, animate or otherwise, to which so 
many people have a rooted, and, apparently, illogical dislike. my 
pet--and only--horror has been beetles. the mere suspicion of a 
harmless, and, i am told, necessary cockroach, being within several 
feet has always made me seriously uneasy. the thought that a great, 
wing beetle--to me, a flying beetle is the horror of horrors!--was 
with me in my bedroom,--goodness alone knew how it had got there!--was 
unendurable. anyone who had beheld me during the next few moments would 
certainly have supposed i was deranged. i turn and twisted, sprang 
from side to side, screw myself into impossible positions, in order 
to obtain a glimpse of the detest visitant,--but in vain. i could 
hear it all the time; but see it--never! the buzz sound was 
continual behind. 

the terror returned,--i began to think that my brain must be softening. 
i dashed to the bed. fling myself on my knees, i try to pray. but 
i was speechless,--words would not come; my thought would not take 
shape. i all at once became conscious, as i struggle to ask help of 
god, that i was wrestle with something evil,--that if i only could 
ask kelp of him, evil would flee. but i could not. i was 
helpless,--overmastered. i hid my face in the bedclothes, cram my 
finger into my ears. but the buzz was behind me all the time. 

i sprang up, striking out, blindly, wildly, right and left, hit 
nothing,--the buzz always came from a point at which, at the moment, 
i was not aiming. 

i tore off my clothes. i had on a lovely frock which i had worn for the 
first time that night; i had had it specially made for the occasion of 
the duchess' ball, and--more especially--in honour of paul's great 
speech. i had said to myself, when i saw my image in a mirror, that it 
was the most exquisite gown i had ever had, that it suit me to 
perfection, and that it should continue in my wardrobe for many a day, 
if only as a souvenir of a memorable night. now, in the madness of my 
terror, all reflection of that sort were forgotten. my only desire was 
to away with it. i tore it off anyhow, letting it fall in rag on the 
floor at my feet. all else that i had on i flung in the same way after 
it; it was a veritable holocaust of dainty garments,--i acting as 
relentless executioner who am, as a rule, so tender with my things. i 
leap upon the bed, switch off the electric light, hurried into bed, 
bury myself, over head and all, deep down between the sheets. 

i had hope that by shut out the light, i might regain my senses. 
that in the dark i might have opportunity for sane reflection. but 
i had made a grievous error. i had exchange bad for worse. the 
dark lent add terrors. the light had not been out five seconds 
before i would have given all that i was worth to be able to switch it 
on again. 

as i cower beneath the bedclothes i heard the buzz sound above my 
head,--the sudden silence of the dark had render it more audible 
than it had been before. the thing, whatever it was, was hover above 
the bed. it came near and nearer; it grew clear and clearer. i felt 
it alight upon the coverlet;--shall i ever forget the sensation with 
which i did feel it? it weigh upon me like a ton of lead. how much of 
the seeming weight was real, and how much imaginary, i cannot pretend 
to say; but that it was much heavy than any beetle i have ever seen 
or heard of, i am sure. 

for a time it was still,--and during that time i doubt if i even drew 
my breath. then i felt it begin to move, in wobble fashion, with 
awkward, ungainly gait, stopping every now and then, as if for rest. i 
was conscious that it was progressing, slowly, yet surely, towards the 
head of the bed. the emotion of horror with which i realise what this 
progression might mean, will be, i fear, with me to the end of my 
life,--not only in dreams, but too often, also, in my waking hours. my 
heart, as the psalmist has it, melt like wax within me, i was 
incapable of movement,--dominated by something as hideous as, and 
infinite more powerful than, the fascination of the serpent. 

when it reach the head of the bed, what i feared--with what a 
fear!--would happen, did happen. it began to find its way inside,--to 
creep between the sheets; the wonder is i did not die! i felt it coming 
near and nearer, inch by inch; i knew that it was upon me, that 
escape there was none; i felt something touch my hair. 

and then oblivion did come to my aid. for the first time in my life i 
swooned. 




chapter xxviii 

the strange story of the man in the street 


i have been anticipate for some week past, that things would become 
exciting,--and they have. but hardly in the way which i foresaw. it is 
the old story of the unexpected happening. sudden event of the most 
extraordinary nature have come crowd on me from the most 
unlooked-for quarters. 

let me try to take them in something like their proper order. 

to begin with, sydney has behave very badly. so badly that it seem 
likely that i shall have to re-cast my whole conception of his 
character. it was nearly nine o'clock this morning when i,--i cannot 
say woke up, because i do not believe that i had really been 
asleep--but when i return to consciousness. i found myself sitting up 
in bed, tremble like some frightened child. what had actually 
happen to me i did not know,--could not guess. i was conscious of an 
overwhelming sense of nausea, and, generally, i was feeling very far 
from well. i endeavour to arrange my thoughts, and to decide upon 
some plan of action. finally, i decided to go for advice and help where 
i had so often gone before,--to sydney atherton. 

i went to him. i told him the whole gruesome story. he saw, he could 
not help but see what a deep impress the event of the night had made 
on me. he heard me to the end with every appearance of sympathy,--and 
then all at once i discover that all the time papa had been conceal 
behind a large screen which was in the room, listen to every word i 
had been uttering. that i was dumfoundered, go without saying. it was 
bad enough in papa, but in sydney it seemed, and it was, such 
treachery. he and i have told each other secret all our lives; it has 
never enter my imagination, as he very well knows, to play him false, 
in one jot or tittle; and i have always understood that, in this sort 
of matter, men pride themselves on their sense of honour being so much 
keen than women's. i told them some plain truths; and i fancy that i 
left them both feeling heartily ashamed of themselves. 

one result the experience had on me,--it wound me up. it had on me the 
revivify effect of a cold douche. i realise that mine was a 
situation in which i should have to help myself. 

when i return home i learned that the man whom i had found in the 
street was himself again, and was as conscious as he was ever likely to 
be. burning with curiosity to learn the nature of the connection which 
exist between paul and him, and what was the meaning of his oracular 
apostrophes, i merely pause to remove my hat before hasten into his 
apartment. 

when he saw me, and heard who i was, the expression of his gratitude 
were painful in their intensity. the tear stream down his cheeks. he 
look to me like a man who had very little life left in him. he look 
weak, and white, and worn to a shadow. probably he never had been 
robust, and it was only too plain that privation had rob him of what 
little strength he had ever had. he was nothing else but skin and bone. 
physical and mental debility was written large all over him. 

he was not bad-looking,--in a milk and watery sort of way. he had pale 
blue eyes and very fair hair, and, i daresay, at one time, had been a 
spruce enough clerk. it was difficult to guess his age, one age so 
rapid under the stress of misfortune, but i should have set him down 
as being about forty. his voice, though faint enough at first, was that 
of an educated man, and as he went on, and gather courage, and became 
more and more in earnest, he spoke with a simple direct which was 
close akin to eloquence. it was a curious story which he had to tell. 

so curious, so astound indeed, that, by the time it was finished, i 
was in such a state of mind, that i could perceive no alternative but 
to forgive sydney, and, in spite of his recent, and scandalous

incredible though it sounded, he spoke like a truthful man!--that paul 
was threaten by some dreadful, and, to me, wholly incomprehensible

i could not move alone. the shadow of the terror of the night was with 
me still, and with that fresh in my recollection how could i hope,

in his own peculiar way; i knew that he was quick, and cool, and 
fertile in resource, and that he show to most advantage in a 
difficult situation; it was possible that he had a conscience, of a 
sort, and that, this time, i might not appeal to it in vain. 

so i sent a servant off to fetch him, helter skelter. 

as luck would have it, the servant return with him within five 
minutes. it appear that he had been lunch with dora grayling, who 
lives just at the end of the street, and the footman had met him coming 
down the steps. i had him shown into my own room. 

'i want you to go to the man whom i found in the street, and listen to 
what he has to say.' 

'with pleasure.' 

'can i trust you?' 

'to listen to what he has to say?--i believe so.' 

'can i trust you to respect my confidence?' 

he was not at all abashed,--i never saw sydney atherton when he was 
abashed. whatever the offence of which he has been guilty, he always

'you can,--i will not breathe a syllable even to papa.' 

'in that case, come! but, you understand, i am go to put to the test

'so!' he exclaimed. 'it's you!'

'i am hardly prepared to go so far as to say that i know him, but, i

the stranger seem uneasy,--as if he found sidney's tone and manner

'i do. you are the man in the street.' 

'precisely. i am that--individual. and you are the man who came through

miss lindon, that i may have a few remark to make to this gentleman

sydney smile that absurd, provoking smile of his,--as if the occasion

'to show that you still repose in me a vestige of your confidence.'

to humour him i accept the chair he offered, though i should have

'my name is robert holt.' 

'that so?--then, mr robert holt,--let her go!'

i let him have his way,--though i knew he was conceal something from

then he said, with the quizzical little air of bland impertinence which

'i presume, mr holt, you have been entertaining us with a novelty in

'i expect nothing. but i have told you the truth. and you know it.' 

this seem to take sydney aback.

i saw that mr holt shuddered.

if the original was more remarkable than the description which he gave

'my blood?'

i let him have his old revolver,--or, rather, i let him have one of
