chapter xxix 

the house on the road from the workhouse 


mr holt look as if he was in somebody else's garments. he was so 
thin, and worn, and wasted, that the suit of clothes which one of the 
men had lent him hung upon him as on a scarecrow. i was almost ashamed 
of myself for have incur a share of the responsibility of taking 
him out of bed. he seem so weak and bloodless that i should not have 
been surprise if he had faint on the road. i had taken care that he 
should eat as much as he could eat before we started--the suggestion of 
starvation which he had convey to one's mind was dreadful!--and i had 
brought a flask of brandy in case of accidents, but, in spite of 
everything, i could not conceal from myself that he would be more at 
home in a sick-bed than in a jolt cab. 

it was not a cheerful drive. there was in sydney's manner towards me an 
air of protection which i instinctive resented,--he appear to be 
regarding me as a careful, and anxious, nurse might regard a 
wrong-headed and disobedient child. conversation distinct languished. 
since sydney seem disposed to patronise me, i was bent on snub 
him. the result was, that the majority of the remark which were 
utter were address to mr holt. 

the cab stopped,--after what had appear to me to be an interminable 
journey. i was rejoice at the prospect of its being at an end. sydney 
put his head out of the window. a short parley with the driver ensued. 

'this is 'ammersmith workhouse, it's a large place, sir,--which part of 
it might you be wanting?' 

sydney appeal to mr holt. he put his head out of the window in his 
turn,--he did not seem to recognise our surroundings at all. 

'we have come a different way,--this is not the way i went; i went 
through hammersmith,--and to the casual ward; i don't see that here.' 

sydney spoke to the cabman. 

'driver, where's the casual ward?' 

'that's the other end, sir.' 

'then take us there.' 

he took us there. then sydney appeal again to mr holt. 

'shall i dismiss the cabman,--or don't you feel equal to walking?' 

'thank you, i feel quite equal to walking,--i think the exercise will 
do me good.' 

so the cabman was dismissed,--a step which we--and i, in 
particular--had subsequent cause to regret. mr holt took his bearings. 
he pointed to a door which was just in front of us. 

'that's the entrance to the casual ward, and that, over it, is the 
window through which the other man threw a stone. i went to the 
right,--back the way i had come.' we went to the right. 'i reach this 
corner.' we had reach a corner. mr holt look about him, 
endeavour to recall the way he had gone. a good many road appear 
to converge at that point, so that he might have wander in either of 
several directions. 

presently he arrive at something like a decision. 

'i think this is the way i went,--i am nearly sure it is.' 

he led the way, with something of an air of dubitation, and we 
followed. the road he had chosen seem to lead to nothing and nowhere. 
we had not gone many yard from the workhouse gate before we were 
confront by something like chaos. in front and on either side of us 
were large space of waste land. at some more or less remote period 
attempt appear to have been made at brick-making,--there were untidy 
stack of bilious-looking brick in evidence. here and there enormous 
weather-stained boards announce that 'this desirable land was to be 
let for building purposes.' the road itself was unfinished. there was 
no pavement, and we had the bare uneven ground for sidewalk. it seemed, 
so far as i could judge, to lose itself in space, and to be swallow 
up by the wilderness of 'desirable land' which lay beyond. in the near 
distance there were house enough, and to spare--of a kind. but they 
were in other roads. in the one in which we actually were, on the 
right, at the end, there was a row of unfurnished carcases, but only 
two building which were in anything like a fit state for occupation. 
one stood on either side, not facing each other,--there was a distance 
between them of perhaps fifty yards. the sight of them had a more 
exciting effect on mr holt than it had on me. he move rapid 
forward,--coming to a standstill in front of the one upon our left, 
which was the near of the pair. 

'this is the house!' he exclaimed. 

he seem almost exhilarated,--i confess that i was depressed. a more 
dismal-looking habitation one could hardly imagine. it was one of those 
dreadful jerry-built house which, while they are still new, look old. 
it had quite possibly only been built a year or two, and yet, owe to 
neglect, or to poverty of construction, or to a combination of the two, 
it was already threaten to tumble down. it was a small place, a 
couple of storey high, and would have been dear--i should think!--at 
thirty pound a year. the window had surely never been wash since 
the house was built,--those on the upper floor seem all either 
cracked or broken. the only sign of occupancy consist in the fact 
that a blind was down behind the window of the room on the ground 
floor. curtains there were none. a low wall ran in front, which had 
apparently at one time been surmount by something in the shape of an 
iron railing,--a rusty piece of metal still remain on one end; but, 
since there was only about a foot between it and the building, which 
was practically built upon the road,--whether the wall was intended to 
ensure privacy, or was merely for ornament, was not clear. 

'this is the house!' repeated mr holt, showing more sign of life than 
i had hitherto seen in him. 

sydney look it up and down,--it apparently appeal to his aesthetic 
sense as little as it did to mine. 

'are you sure?' 

'i am certain.' 

'it seem empty.' 

'it seem empty to me that night,--that is why i got into it in search 
of shelter.' 

'which is the window which serve you as a door?' 

'this one.' mr holt pointed to the window on the ground floor,--the one 
which was screen by a blind. 'there was no sign of a blind when i 
first saw it, and the sash was up,--it was that which caught my eye.' 

once more sydney survey the place, in comprehensive fashion, from 
roof to basement,--then he scrutinising regard mr holt. 

'you are quite sure this is the house? it might be awkward if you 
prove mistaken. i am go to knock at the door, and if it turn out 
that that mysterious acquaintance of yours do not, and never has 
live here, we might find an explanation difficult.' 

'i am sure it is the house,--certain! i know it,--i feel it here,--and 
here.' 

mr holt touched his breast, and his forehead. his manner was distinct 
odd. he was trembling, and a fevered expression had come into his eyes. 
sydney glance at him, for a moment, in silence. then he bestow his 
attention upon me. 

'may i ask if i may rely upon your preserve your presence of mind?' 

the mere question ruffle my plumes.

chapter xxx 

the singular behaviour of mr holt 


i was standing in the middle of the room, sydney was between the door

and i was alone. 




chapter xxxi 

the terror by day 


my first impulse, after sydney's disappearance, was to laugh. why 
should he display anxiety on my behalf merely because i was to be the 
sole occupant of an otherwise empty house for a few minutes more or 
less,--and in broad daylight too! to say the least, the anxiety seem 
unwarranted. 

i linger at the gate, for a moment or two, wonder what was at the 
bottom of mr holt's singular proceedings, and what sydney really 
propose to gain by acting as a spy upon his wanderings. then i turn 
to re-enter the house. as i did so, another problem suggest itself to 
my mind,--what connection, of the slightest importance, could a man in 
paul lessingham's position have with the eccentric being who had 
establish himself in such an unsatisfactory dwelling-place? mr holt's 
story i had only dim understood,--it struck me that it would require 
a deal of understanding. it was more like a farrago of nonsense, an 
outcome of delirium, than a plain statement of solid facts. to tell the 
truth, sydney had taken it more seriously than i expected. he seem to 
see something in it which i emphatically did not. what was double dutch 
to me, seem clear as print to him. so far as i could judge, he 
actually had the presumption to imagine that paul--my paul!--paul 
lessingham!--the great paul lessingham!--was mixed up in the very 
mysterious adventure of poor, weak-minded, hysterical mr holt, in a 
manner which was hardly to his credit. 

of course, any idea of the kind was purely and simply balderdash. 
exactly what bee sydney had got in his bonnet, i could not guess. but i 
did know paul. only let me find myself face to face with the fantastic 
author of mr holt's weird tribulations, and i, a woman, single-handed, 
would do my best to show him that whoever play prank with paul 
lessingham trifle with edge tools. 

i had return to that historical front room which, according to mr 
holt, had been the scene of his most disastrous burglarious entry. 
whoever had furnish it had had original notions of the resources of 
modern upholstery. there was not a table in the place,--no chair or 
couch, nothing to sit down upon except the bed. on the floor there was 
a marvellous carpet which was apparently of eastern manufacture. it was 
so thick, and so pliant to the tread, that moving over it was like 
walking on thousand-year-old turf. it was woven in gorgeous colours, 
and cover with-- 

when i discover what it actually was cover with, i was conscious of 
a disagreeable sense of surprise. 

it was cover with beetles! 

all over it, with only a few inch of space between each, were 
representations of some peculiar kind of beetle,--it was the same 
beetle, over, and over, and over. the artist had woven his undesirable 
subject into the warp and woof of the material with such cunning skill 
that, as one continue to gaze, one began to wonder if by any 
possibility the creature could be alive. 

in spite of the soft of the texture, and the art--of a kind!--which 
had been display in the workmanship, i rapid arrive at the 
conclusion that it was the most uncomfortable carpet i had ever seen. i 
wag my finger at the repeated portrayal of the--to me!--unspeakable 
insect. 

'if i had discover that you were there before sydney went, i think it 
just possible that i should have hesitate before i let him go.' 

then there came a revulsion of feeling. i shook myself. 

'you ought to be ashamed of yourself, marjorie lindon, to even think 
such nonsense. are you all nerves and morbid imaginings,--you who have 
pride yourself on being so strong-minded! a pretty sort you are to do 
battle for anyone.--why, they're only make-believes!' 

half involuntarily, i drew my foot over one of the creatures. of 
course, it was nothing but imagination; but i seem to feel it squelch 
beneath my shoe. it was disgusting. 

'come!' i cried. 'this won't do! as sydney would phrase it,--am i go 
to make an idiot of myself?' 

i turn to the window,--looking at my watch. 

'it's more than five minutes ago since sydney went. that companion of 
mine ought to be already on the way. i'll go and see if he is coming.' 

i went to the gate. there was not a soul in sight. it was with such a 
distinct sense of disappointment that i perceive this was so, that i 
was in two mind what to do. to remain where i was, looking, with 
gape eyes, for the policeman, or the cabman, or whoever it was sydney

unmistakable reluctance to return within the house. 

common sense, or what i took for common sense, however, triumphed, and, 
after loiter for another five minutes, i did go in again. 

this time, ignoring, to the best of my ability, the beetle on the 
floor, i proceed to expend my curiosity--and occupy my thoughts--in 
an examination of the bed. it only need a very cursory examination, 
however, to show that the seeming bed was, in reality, none at all,--or 
if it was a bed after the manner of the eastern it certainly was not 
after the fashion of the britons. there was no framework,--nothing to 
represent the bedstead. it was simply a heap of rug pile apparently 
indiscriminate upon the floor. a huge mass of them there seem to 
be; of all sorts, and shapes, and sizes,--and material too. 

the top one was of white silk,--in quality, exquisite. it was of huge 
size, yet, with a little compression, one might almost have pass it 
through the proverbial wedding ring. so far as space admitted i spread 
it out in front of me. in the middle was a picture,--whether it was 
embroider on the substance or woven in it, i could not quite make

by degrees, i realise that the lurid hue were meant for flames,--and, 
when one had got so far, one perceive that they were by no means badly 
imitate either. then the meaning of the thing dawn on me,--it was a 
representation of a human sacrifice. in its way, as ghastly a piece of 
realism as one could see. 

on the right was the majestic seat figure of a goddess. her hand 
were cross upon her knees, and she was naked from her waist upwards. 
i fancy it was meant for isis. on her brow was perch a 
gaily-apparelled beetle--that ubiquitous beetle!--forming a bright spot

an enormous fiery furnace. in the very heart of the flame was an 
altar. on the altar was a naked white woman being burn alive. there 
could be no doubt as to her being alive, for she was secure by chain

of which she was avail herself to contort and twist her body into 
shape which were horrible suggestive of the agony which she was 
enduring,--the artist, indeed, seem to have exhaust his powers in 
his effort to convey a vivid impression of the pains which were 
torment her. 

'a pretty picture, on my word! a pleasant taste in art the garniture 
of this establishment suggest! the person who likes to live with this 
kind of thing, especially as a covering to his bed, must have his own 
notions as to what constitute agreeable surroundings.' 

as i continue staring at the thing, all at once it seem as if the 
woman on the altar moved. it was preposterous, but she appear to 
gather her limb together, and turn half over. 

'what can be the matter with me? am i go mad? she can't be moving!' 

if she wasn't, then certainly something was,--she was lift right into 
the air. an idea occur to me. i snatch the rug aside. 

the mystery was explained! 

a thin, yellow, wrinkle hand was protrude from amidst the heap of 
rugs,--it was its action which had cause the seeming movement of the 
figure on the altar. i stared, confounded. the hand was follow by an 
arm; the arm by a shoulder; the shoulder by a head,--and the most 
awful, hideous, wicked-looking face i had ever picture even in my most 
dreadful dreams. a pair of baleful eyes were glaring up at mine. 

i understood the position in a flash of startle amazement. 

sydney, in following mr holt, had start on a wild goose chase after 
all. i was alone with the occupant of that mysterious house,--the chief 
actor in mr holt's astound tale. he had been hidden in the heap of 
rug all the while.

chapter xxxii 

a new client 


on the afternoon of friday, june 2, 18--, i was enter in my

'i am.' 

'i believe that i have not had the honour of meeting you before, mr 
champnell, but with your father, the earl of glenlivet, i have the 
pleasure of some acquaintance.' 

i bowed. he look at me, fixedly, as if he were try to make out 
what sort of man i was. 'you are very young, mr champnell.' 

'i have been told that an eminent offender in that respect once 
assert that youth is not of necessity a crime.' 

'and you have chosen a singular profession,--one in which one hardly 
looks for juvenility.' 

'you yourself, mr lessingham, are not old. in a statesman one expect 
grey hairs.--i trust that i am sufficient ancient to be able to do 
you service.' 

he smiled. 

'i think it possible. i have heard of you more than once, mr champnell, 
always to your advantage. my friend, sir john seymour, was telling me, 
only the other day, that you have recently conduct for him some 
business, of a very delicate nature, with much skill and tact; and he 
warm advise me, if ever i found myself in a predicament, to come to 
you. i find myself in a predicament now.' 

again i bowed. 

'a predicament, i fancy, of an altogether unparalleled sort. i take it 
that anything i may say to you will be as though it were said to a 
father confessor.' 

'you may rest assured of that.' 

'good.--then, to make the matter clear to you i must begin by telling 
you a story,--if i may trespass on your patience to that extent. i will 
endeavour not to be more verbose than the occasion requires.' 

i offer him a chair, place it in such a position that the light 
from the window would have shone full upon his face. with the calmest 
possible air, as if unconscious of my design, he carry the chair to 
the other side of my desk, twist it right round before he sat on 
it,--so that now the light was at his back and on my face. crossing his 
legs, clasp his hand about his knee, he sat in silence for some 
moments, as if turning something over in his mind. he glance round the 
room. 

'i suppose, mr champnell, that some singular tale have been told in 
here.' 

'some very singular tale indeed. i am never appall by singularity. 
it is my normal atmosphere.' 

'and yet i should be disposed to wager that you have never listen to 
so strange a story as that which i am about to tell you now. so 
astonishing, indeed, is the chapter in my life which i am about to open 
out to you, that i have more than once had to take myself to task, and 
fit the incident together with mathematics accuracy in order to 
assure myself of its perfect truth.' 

he paused. there was about his demeanour that suggestion of reluctance 
which i not uncommonly discover in individual who are about to take 
the skeleton from their cupboard and parade them before my eyes. his 
next remark seem to point to the fact that he perceive what was 
passing through my thoughts. 

'my position is not render easy by the circumstance that i am not 
of a communicative nature. i am not in sympathy with the spirit of the 
age which crave for personal advertisement. i hold that the private 
life even of a public man should be held inviolate. i resent, with 
peculiar bitterness, the attempt of pry eyes to peer into matter 
which, as it seem to me, concern myself alone. you must, therefore, 
bear with me, mr champnell, if i seem awkward in disclose to you 
certain incident in my career which i had hope would continue lock

carry to the grave. i am sure you will suffer me to stand excuse if 
i frankly admit that it is only an irresistible chain of incident 
which has constrained me to make of you a confidant.' 

'my experience tell me, mr lessingham, that no one ever do come to 
me until they are compelled. in that respect i am regard as something 
worse even than a medical man.' 

a wintry smile flit across his features,--it was clear that he 
regard me as a good deal worse than a medical man. presently he began 
to tell me one of the most remarkable tale which even i had heard. as 
he proceed i understood how strong, and how natural, had been his 
desire for reticence. on the mere score of credibility he must have 
greatly prefer to have kept his own counsel. for my part i own, 
unreservedly, that i should have deem the tale incredible had it been 
told me by tom, dick, or harry, instead of by paul lessingham.

what came of look through a lattice 


he began in accent which halt not a little. by degree his voice

'i am not yet forty. so when i tell you that twenty years ago i was a

saw the name on the corner as i turn into it, and it has left an

'it was a narrow street, and, of course, a dirty one, ill-lit, and,

'i paused. i stood awhile to listen. 

'there was an open window on my right, which was screen by lattice

mr lessingham stopped. a stream of recollection seem to come flood

'"entrez, monsieur!--soye le bienvenu!" 

'i was a little tired. rather curious as to whereabouts i was,--the

after twenty years 


'how i reach the open air i cannot tell you,--i do not know. i have a 
confuse recollection of rush through vaulted passages, through 
endless corridors, of trample over people who try to arrest my 
passage,--and the rest is blank. 

'when i again came to myself i was lie in the house of an america 
missionary name clements. i had been found, at early dawn, stark

appearances i must have wander for miles, all through the night. 
whence i had come, or whither i was going, none could tell,--i could 
not tell myself. for week i hover between life and death. the 
kindness of mr and mrs clement was not to be measured by words. i was 
brought to their house a penniless, helpless, batter stranger, and 
they gave me all they had to offer, without money and without 
price,--with no expectation of an earthly reward. let no one pretend 
that there is no christian charity under the sun. the debt i owe that 
man and woman i was never able to repay. before i was properly myself 
again, and in a position to offer some adequate testimony of the 
gratitude i felt, mrs clement was dead, drown during an excursion on 
the nile, and her husband had departed on a missionary expedition into 
central africa, from which he never returned. 

'although, in a measure, my physical health returned, for month after 
i had left the roof of my hospitable hosts, i was in a state of

name. and, when that stage had passed, and i began to move more freely

visited, at all hour of the day and night, by frightful--i know not 
whether to call them visions, they were real enough to me, but since 
they were visible to no one but myself, perhaps that is the word which 
best describe them. their presence invariable plunge me into a state 
of abject terror, against which i was unable to even make a show of 
fighting. to such an extent did they embitter my existence, that i 
voluntary placed myself under the treatment of an expert in mental 
pathology. for a considerable period of time i was under his constant 
supervision, but the visitation were as inexplicable to him as they 
were to me. 

'by degrees, however, they became rare and rarer, until at last i 
flatter myself that i had once more become as other men. after an 
interval, to make sure, i devoted myself to politics. thenceforward i 
have lived, as they phrase it, in the public eye. private life, in any 
peculiar sense of the term, i have had none.' 

mr lessingham ceased. his tale was not uninteresting, and, to say the

'i presume, mr lessingham, that all this is but a prelude to the play. 
at present i do not see where it is that i come in.' 

still for some seconds he was silent. when he spoke his voice was grave 
and sombre, as if he were burden by a weight of woe. 

'unfortunately, as you put it, all this has been but a prelude to the

i smiled,--the compliment was a pointed one. 

'i hope your estimate of me is not too high.' 

'i hope not,--for my sake, as well as for your own. i have heard great

his words arouse my curiosity. i was conscious of feeling more

'i will do my best for you. man can do no more. only give my best a

'i will. at once.' 

he look at me long and earnestly. then, leaning forward, he said,

'the fact is, mr champnell, that quite recently event have happen

'that, again, i cannot tell you.' 

'what do you mean?' 

'what i say. each time, when i recovered, the thing had vanished.' 

'sheet of paper and all?' 

'apparently,--though on that point i could not be positive. you will

i began to suspect that this was a case rather for a doctor than for a

'don't you think it is possible, mr lessingham, that you have been

'i thought so myself; i may say that i almost hope so. but wait till i 
have finished. you will find that there is no loophole in that

he appear to be recall event in their due order. his manner was

'the night before last, on return home, i found in my study a

'a stranger?' 

'yes.--in other words, a burglar.' 

'a burglar?--i see.--go on.' 

he had paused. his demeanour was becoming odd and odder. 

'on my entry he was engaged in force an entry into my bureau. i need

'i mean simply what i say. you must understand that this was no

mr lessingham open his mouth,--and shut it. a marked change took

his voice was dry and husky,--unlike his usual silvern tones. after an

'i suppose, mr lessingham, that there is no doubt that this mysterious

'did your servant see him?' 

'some of them,--yes. then there is the evidence of the bureau. the

'if matter stand as i fear they do, he might make a very serious

'he escape by the much more prosaic method of dashing through the

'into whose arms,--a constable's?' 

'no; into mr atherton's,--sydney atherton's.' 

'the inventor?' 

'the same.--do you know him?'

'mr atherton's reason were his own. he did not stop him, and, so far

he drew his chair close to me with an air of brisk which had been

however, i was not long to remain in darkness. in half a dozen

'mr atherton, like myself, has been a postulant for miss lindon's hand. 
because i have succeed where he has failed, he has chosen to be

that is an eventuality which i wish to avoid. my own conviction is that

and where, this person is, to drag her!--or him;--out into the light of

individual of whom you speak,--has he told you so?' 

'practically,--yes.' 

'i know atherton well. in his not infrequent moments of excitement he 
is apt to use strong language, but it go no further. i believe him to

under any circumstances whatever. if i go to him, armed with

'then go to him at once.' 

'good. i will. the result i will communicate to you.' 

i rose from my seat. as i did so, someone rush into the outer office 
with a din and a clatter. andrews' voice, and another, became 
distinct audible,--andrews' apparently raise in vigorous

own particular sanctum was thrown open with a crash, and mr sydney 
atherton himself came dashing in,--evidently conspicuous under the 
influence of one of those not infrequent 'moments of excitement' of 
which i had just been speaking. 




chapter xxxv 

a bring of tidings 


atherton did not wait to see who might or might not be present, but,

i endeavour to call his attention to mr lessingham's presence,--but

'my dear fellow--'

in his harum-scarum haste mr lessingham had gone unnoticed. now that

flattering. 

'oh!--it's you, is it?--what the deuce are you do here?' 

before lessingham could reply to this most unceremonious query,

lessingham, not unnatural nonplus by the other's curious conduct,

'marjorie lindon!' 

'marjorie lindon?' 

lessingham paused. he was evidently ask himself what the inquiry

his words were incomprehensible enough to stand in copious need of

mr lessingham did not give him his hand. atherton took it,--and gave it

if the first paroxysm of his passion had passed, lessingham was still 
sufficient stern. 

'be so good as not to trifle, mr atherton. if what you say is correct,

what the tidings were 


three in a hansom cab is not, under all circumstances, the most

incessant indulge did not sufficient attract public notice to

had speed not been of primary importance i should have insist on the 
transference of the expedition to the somewhat wide limit of a

we were already go much fast than, legally, we ought to have

despite the splinter glass in the window frame, stuffy. fragment of 
glass kept company with the dust on the floor, together with a choice 
collection of stones, brickbats, and other missiles,--which not 
improbable were the cause of their being there. in the corner stood a 
cupboard,--but a momentary examination show that that was as bare as 
the other. the door at the side, which sydney had left wide open, 
open on to a closet, and that was empty. i glance up,--there was no 
trap door which led to the roof. no practicable nook or cranny, in 
which a living being could lie concealed, was anywhere at hand. 

i return to sydney's shoulder to tell the cabman so. 

'there is no place in which anyone could hide, and there is no one in 
either of the rooms,--you must have been mistaken, driver.' 

the man wax wroth. 

'don't tell me! how could i come to think i saw something when i

'one's eyes are apt to play us tricks;--how could you see what wasn't 
there?' 

'that's what i want to know. as i drove up, before you told me to stop, 
i saw him look through the window,--the one at which you are. he'd 
got his nose glue to the broken pane, and was staring as hard as he 
could stare. when i pull up, off he started,--i saw him get up off 
his knees, and go to the back of the room. when the gentleman took to 
knocking, back he came,--to the same old spot, and flop down on his 
knees. i didn't know what caper you was up to,--you might be bum 
bailiff for all i knew!--and i supposed that he wasn't so anxious to 
let you in as you might be to get inside, and that was why he didn't 
take no notice of your knocking, while all the while he kept a eye on 
what was go on. when you go round to the back, up he get again, 
and i reckon that he was go to meet yer, and perhaps give yer a 
bit of his mind, and that presently i should hear a shindy, or that 
something would happen. but when you pull up the blind downstairs, to 
my surprise back he come once more. he shove his old nose right 
through the smash in the pane, and wag his old head at me like a 
chatter magpie. that didn't seem to me quite the civil thing to 
do,--i hadn't done no harm to him; so i give you the office, and let 
you know that he was there. but for you to say that he wasn't there, 
and never had been,--blimey! that cop the biscuit. if he wasn't there, 
all i can say is i ain't here, and my 'orse ain't here, and my cab 
ain't neither,--damn it!--the house ain't here, and nothing ain't!' 

he settled himself on his perch with an air of the most extreme ill 
usage,--he had been standing up to tell his tale. that the man was 
serious was unmistakable. as he himself suggested, what inducement 
could he have had to tell a lie like that? that he believe himself to 
have seen what he declared he saw was plain. but, on the other hand, 
what could have become--in the space of fifty seconds!--of his 'old 
gent'? 

atherton put a question. 

'what did he look like,--this old gent of yours?' 

'well, that i shouldn't hardly like to say. it wasn't much of his face 
i could see, only his face and his eyes,--and they wasn't pretty. he 
kept a thing over his head all the time, as if he didn't want too much 
to be seen.' 

'what sort of a thing?' 

'why,--one of them cloak sort of things, like them arab bloke use to 
wear what use to be at earl's court exhibition,--you know!' 

this piece of information seem to interest my companion more than 
anything he had said before. 

'a burnoose do you mean?' 

'how am i to know what the thing's called? i ain't up in foreign 
languages,--'tain't likely! all i know that them arab bloke what was 
at earl's court use to walk about in them all over the 
place,--sometimes they wore them over their heads, and sometimes they 
didn't. in fact if you'd ask me, instead of try to make out as i 
see double, or things what was only inside my own noddle, or something

you about was a arab bloke,--when he get off his knee to sneak away 
from the window, i could see that he had his cloak thing, what was over 
his head, wrap all round him.' 

mr lessingham turn to me, all quiver with excitement. 

'i believe that what he say is true!' 

'then where can this mysterious old gentleman have got to,--can you 
suggest an explanation? it is strange, to say the least of it, that the

'some devil's trick has been played,--i know it, i feel it!--my 
instinct tell me so!' 

i stared. in such a matter one hardly expect a man of paul 
lessingham's stamp to talk of 'instinct.' atherton stare too. then, on 
a sudden, he burst out, 

'by the lord, i believe the apostle's right,--the whole place reek to 
me of hankey-pankey,--it did as soon as i put my nose inside. in 
matter of prestidigitation, champnell, we western are among the 
rudiments,--we've everything to learn,--orientals leave us at the post. 
if their civilisation's what we're pleased to call extinct, their

he move towards the door. as he went he slipped, or seem to, all but

'something trip me up,--what's this?' he was stamp on the floor 
with his foot. 'here's a board loose. come and lend me a hand, one of

i went to his aid. as he said, a board in the floor was loose. his

have remove it, we peer into the cavity it disclosed. 

there was something there. 

'why,' cry atherton 'it's a woman's clothing!' 




chapter xxxviii 

the rest of the find 


it was a woman's clothing, beyond a doubt, all thrown in anyhow,--as if 
the person who had placed it there had been in a desperate hurry. an 
entire outfit was there, shoes, stockings, body linen, corsets, and 
all,--even to hat, gloves, and hairpins;--these latter were mixed up 
with the rest of the garment in strange confusion. it seem plain 
that whoever had worn those clothes had been strip to the skin. 

lessingham and sydney stare at me in silence as i drag them out and 
laid them on the floor. the dress was at the bottom,--it was an alpaca, 
of a pretty shade in blue, bedeck with lace and ribbons, as is the 
fashion of the hour, and line with sea-green silk. it had perhaps been 
a 'charming confection' once--and that a very recent one!--but now it 
was all soil and crease and torn and tumbled. the two spectator 
made a simultaneous pounce at it as i brought it to the light. 

'my god!' cry sydney, 'it's marjorie's!--she was wearing it when i 
saw her last!' 

'it's marjorie's!' gasp lessingham,--he was clutch at the ruin 
costume, staring at it like a man who has just received sentence of 
death. 'she wore it when she was with me yesterday,--i told her how it 
suit her, and how pretty it was!' 

there was silence,--it was an eloquent find; it spoke for itself. the 
two men gaze at the heap of feminine glories,--it might have been the 
most wonderful sight they ever had seen. lessingham was the first to 
speak,--his face had all at once grown grey and haggard. 

'what has happen to her?' 

i reply to his question with another. 

'are you sure this is miss linden's dress?' 

'i am sure,--and were proof needed, here it is.' 

he had found the pocket, and was turning out the contents. there was a 
purse, which contained money and some visiting cards on which were her

handkerchief, with her initial in a corner. the question of ownership 
was placed beyond a doubt. 

'you see,' said lessingham, exhibit the money which was in the

thirty pound in all.' 

atherton, who had been turning over the accumulation of rubbish between 
the joists, proclaim another find. 

'here are her rings, and watch, and a bracelet,--no, it certainly do 
not look as if theft had been an object.' 

lessingham was glower at him with knit brows. 

'i have to thank you for this.' 

sydney was unwonted meek. 

'you are hard on me, lessingham, hard than i deserve,--i had rather 
have thrown away my own life than have suffer misadventure to have 
come to her.' 

'yours are idle words. had you not meddle this would not have

to me with your life's blood.' 

'let it be so,' said sydney. 'i am content. if hurt has come to 
marjorie, god know that i am willing enough that death should come to

while they wrangled, i continue to search. a little to one side, under 
the flooring which was still intact, i saw something gleam. by 
stretch out my hand, i could just manage to reach it,--it was a long 
plait of woman's hair. it had been cut off at the roots,--so close to 
the head in one place that the scalp itself had been cut, so that the 
hair was clot with blood. 

they were so occupy with each other that they took no notice of me. i 
had to call their attention to my discovery. 

'gentlemen, i fear that i have here something which will distress 
you,--is not this miss lindon's hair?' 

they recognise it on the instant. lessingham, snatch it from my

'this is mine,--i shall at least have something.' he spoke with a

as i live, i will devote my all,--money, time, reputation!--to gain

atherton chime in. 

'to that i say, amen!' he lift his hand. 'god is my witness!' 

'it seem to me, gentlemen, that we move too fast,--to my mind it do 
not by any means of necessity point to murder. on the contrary, i doubt 
if murder has been done. indeed, i don't mind own that i have a 
theory of my own which points all the other way.' 

lessingham caught me by the sleeve. 

'mr champnell, tell me your theory.' 

'i will, a little later. of course it may be altogether wrong;--though 
i fancy it is not; i will explain my reason when we come to talk of

'i vote for tear up every board in the house!' cry sydney. 'and

the while from some peephole of his own.' 

we examine the entire house, methodically, so far as we were able, 
inch by inch. not another board prove loose,--to lift those which were

the cabman's 'old gent' remain as much a mystery as ever, and no 
further trace had been discover of miss lindon. 

atherton made no effort to disguise his chagrin. 

'now what's to be done? there seem to be just nothing in the place at

'in that case i would suggest that you should stay and look for it. the

sort is, for the moment, of paramount importance; and i propose to

the way.' 

i had observed, on our arrival, that the road only contained two house 
which were in anything like a finished state,--that which we were in, 
and another, some fifty or sixty yard further down, on the opposite

'i will come with you,' said mr lessingham.

over there,--you keep an eye on this one. and if you see a sign of

a yell. i shall be on the lookout, and i'll be with you before you can 
say jack robinson.' 

'you bet i'll yell,--i'll raise the hair right off you.' the fellow 
grinned. 'but i don't know if you gents are hire me by the day,--i 
want to change my horse; he ought to have been in his stable a couple 
of hour ago.' 

'never mind your horse,--let him rest a couple of hour extra to-morrow 
to make up for those he has lost to-day. i'll take care you don't lose 
anything by this little job,--or your horse either.--by the way, look 
here,--this will be better than yelling.' 

taking a revolver out of his trousers' pocket he hand it up to the 
grin driver. 

'if that old gent of yours do appear, you have a pop at him,--i shall 
hear that easy than a yell. you can put a bullet through him if you 
like,--i give you my word it won't be murder.' 

'i don't care if it is,' declared the cabman, handle the weapon like 
one who was familiar with arms of precision. 'i use to fancy my 
revolver shoot when i was with the colours, and if i do get a chance 
i'll put a shot through the old hunks, if only to prove to you that i'm 
no liar.' 

whether the man was in earnest or not i could not tell,--nor whether 
atherton meant what he said in answer. 

'if you shoot him i'll give you fifty pounds.' 

'all right!' the driver laughed. 'i'll do my best to earn that fifty!'

miss louisa coleman 


that the house over the way was tenant was plain to all the

louisa coleman, and i'm the owner of this house, and you can't deceive 
me,--i saw you come in, and you're in now, and if you don't come and 
speak to me this moment i'll have the police." 

'all of a sudden, when i was least expect it, and was hammer my 
very hardest at the pane, up go the blind, and up go the window 
too, and the most awful-looking creature ever i heard of, not to 
mention seeing, put his head right into my face,--he was more like a 
hideous baboon than anything else, let alone a man. i was struck all of 
a heap, and plump down on the little wall, and all but tumble head 
over heel backwards, and he start shrieking, in a sort of a kind of 
english, and in such a voice as i'd never heard the like,--it was like 
a rusty steam engine. 

'"go away! go away! i don't want you! i will not have you,--never! you 
have your fifty pounds,--you have your money,--that is the whole of 
you,--that is all you want! you come to me no more!--never!--never no 
more!--or you be sorry!--go away!" 

'i did go away, and that as fast as ever my leg would carry me,--what 
with his looks, and what with his voice, and what with the way that he 
went on, i was nothing but a mass of trembling. as for answer him 
back, or give him a piece of my mind, as i had meant to, i wouldn't 
have done it not for a thousand pounds. i don't mind confessing, 
between you and me, that i had to swallow four cup of tea, right 
straight away, before my nerves was steady. 

'"well," i say to myself, when i did feel, as it might be, a little 
more easy, "you never have let that house before, and now you've let it 
with a vengeance,--so you have. if that there new tenant of yours isn't 
the greatest villain that ever went unhung it must be because he's got 
near relations what's as bad as himself,--because two family like his 
i'm sure there can't be. a nice sort of arab party to have sleep 
over the road he is!" 

'but after a time i cool down, as it were,--because i'm one of them 
sort as likes to see on both side of a question. "after all," i say 
to myself, "he has paid his rent, and fifty pound is fifty pounds,--i 
doubt if the whole house is worth much more, and he can't do much 
damage to it whatever he does." 

'i shouldn't have minded, so far as that went, if he'd set fire to the 
place, for, between ourselves, it's insured for a good bit over its 
value. so i decided that i'd let things be as they were, and see how 
they went on. but from that hour to this i've never spoken to the man, 
and never want to, and wouldn't, not of my own free will, not for a 
shilling a time,--that face of his will haunt me if i live till noah, 
as the saying is. i've seen him go in and out at all hour of the 
day and night,--that arab party's a mystery if ever there was one,--he 
always go tear along as if he's flying for his life. lot of 
people have come to the house, all sort and kinds, men and 
women--they've been mostly women, and even little children. i've seen 
them hammer and hammer at that front door, but never a one have i seen 
let in,--or yet seen taken any notice of, and i think i may say, and 
yet tell no lie, that i've scarcely took my eye off the house since 
he's been inside it, over and over again in the middle of the night 
have i got up to have a look, so that i've not miss much that has 
took place. 

'what's puzzle me is the noise that's come from the house. sometimes 
for days together there's not been a sound, it might have been a house 
of the dead; and then, all through the night, there've been yell and 
screeches, squawk and screams,--i never heard nothing like it. i have 
thought, and more than once, that the devil himself must be in that 
front room, let alone all the rest of his demons. and as for 
cats!--where they've come from i can't think. i didn't use to notice 
hardly a cat in the neighbourhood till that there arab party 
came,--there isn't much to attract them; but since he came there's been 
regiments. sometimes at night there's been troops about the place, 
screech like mad,--i've wish them farther, i can tell you. that 
arab party must be fond of 'em. i've seen them inside the house, at the 
windows, upstairs and downstairs, as it seem to me, a dozen at a time.

what miss coleman saw through the window

i was meek silent;--plainly, if she was to talk, every one else must

'during the last few days there have been some queer goings on over the

always have been queer enough. that arab party has been flit about

she paused,--to fix her eyes on lessingham. she apparently observe his

'now i'm coming to this afternoon.' 

i thought it was about time,--though for the life of me, i did not dare

'well, it might have been three, or it might have been half past,

at this point i felt that at all hazard i must interpose a question.

she took it better than i fear she might.

for the moment, so far as i was concerned, the query was unanswerable.

i shirk the question. 

'whatever it is, it is our business to prevent his do it.' 

'and where do you think they have been taken?' 

'that it will be our immediate business to endeavour to discover,--and 
here, at any rate, we are at waterloo.'

three singles! then my instinct had told me rightly.

mr stone's eyes twinkled.

we were shown into bellingham's office to await result lessingham

'passengers by 7.30 southampton, on arrival of train, complain of

the wild tale he had told me of that cairene inferno, oddly enough--yet

three persons,--two sister and their brother, who was younger than 
themselves, member of a decent english family, were go on a trip

a point on it--foolhardy. the evening after their arrival in cairo, by

they went,--but they never returned. or, rather the two girl never

re-appearance, but as there were no relations, nor even friends of

anyhow, nothing was discovered. their widowed mother, alone in england, 
wonder how it was that beyond the receipt of a brief wire,

whole town and neighbourhood was turn pretty well upside down. but

authority might just as well have left the mystery of their

however, some three month afterwards a youth was brought to the

one is not always, even in strict professional matters, influence by

a capless, wildly excited young ragamuffin came dashing in at the

'there's been murder done--it's right enough!--there 'as!--up at mrs

help i felt persuaded.

he think that he can do it.' 

the driver lean over his engine, rubbing his hand with the usual 
oily rag. he was a short, wiry man with grey hair and a grizzled 
moustache, with about him that bearing of semi-humorous, frank-faced 
resolution which one note about engine-drivers as a class. 

'we ought to do it, the gradient are against us, but it's a clear 
night and there's no wind. the only thing that will stop us will be if 
there's any shunt on the road, or any luggage trains; of course, if 
we are blocked, we are blocked, but the inspector say he'll clear the 
way for us.' 

'yes,' said the inspector, 'i'll clear the way. i've wire down the 
road already.' 

atherton broke in. 

'driver, if you get us into bedford within five minutes of the arrival 
of the mail there'll be a five-pound note to divide between your mate 
and you.' 

the driver grinned. 

'we'll get you there in time, sir, if we have to go clear through the 
shunters. it isn't often we get a chance of a five-pound note for a run 
to bedford, and we'll do our best to earn it.' 

the fireman wave his hand in the rear. 

'that's right, sir!' he cried. 'we'll have to trouble you for that 
five-pound note.' 

so soon as we were clear of the station it began to seem probable that, 
as the fireman put it, atherton would be 'troubled.' journey in a 
train which consist of a single carriage attach to an engine which 
is flying at topmost speed is a very different business from being an 
occupant of an ordinary train which is travel at ordinary express 
rates. i had discover that for myself before. that night it was 
impress on me more than ever. a tyro--or even a nervous 
'season'--might have been excuse for expect at every moment we were 
go to be derailed. it was hard to believe that the carriage had any 
springs,--it rock and swung, and jog and jolted. of smooth 
travel had we none. talk was out of the question;--and for that, 
i, personally, was grateful. quite apart from the difficulty we 
experienced in keeping our seats--and when every moment our position 
was being alter and we were jerk backwards and forward up and 
down, this way and that, that was a business which require care,--the 
noise was deafening. it was as though we were being pursue by a legion 
of shrieking, bellowing, rage demons. 

'george!' shriek atherton, 'he do mean to earn that fiver. i hope 
i'll be alive to pay it him!' 

he was only at the other end of the carriage, but though i could see by 
the distortion of his visage that he was shouting at the top of his 
voice,--and he has a voice,--i only caught here and there a word or two 
of what he was saying. i had to make sense of the whole. 

lessingham's contortion were a study. few of that large multitude of 
person who are acquaint with him only by means of the portrait 
which have appear in the illustrate papers, would then have 
recognise the rising statesman. yet i believe that few things could 
have better fallen in with his mood than that wild travelling. he might 
have been almost shaken to pieces,--but the very severity of the 
shake serve to divert his thought from the one dread topic which 
threaten to absorb them to the exclusion of all else beside. then 
there was the tonic influence of the element of risk. the pick-me-up 
effect of a spice of peril. actual danger there quite probably was 
none; but there very really seem to be. and one thing was absolutely 
certain, that if we did come to smash while go at that speed we 
should come to as everlasting smash as the heart of man could by any 
possibility desire. it is probable that the knowledge that this was so 
warm the blood in lessingham's veins. at any rate as--to use what in 
this case, was simply a form of speech--i sat and watch him, it 
seem to me that he was get a firm hold of the strength which 
had all but escape him, and that with every jog and jolt he was 
becoming more and more of a man. 

on and on we went dashing, clashing, smashing, roaring, rumbling. 
atherton, who had been endeavour to peer through the window, 
strained his lung again in the effort to make himself audible. 

'where the devil are we?' 

look at my watch i scream back at him. 

'it's nearly one, so i suppose we're somewhere in the neighbourhood of 
luton.--hollo! what's the matter?' 

that something was the matter seem certain. there was a shrill 
whistle from the engine. in a second we were conscious--almost too 
conscious--of the application of the westinghouse brake. of all the 
jolt that was ever jolted! the mere reverberation of the carriage 
threaten to resolve our body into their component parts. feeling 
what we felt then help us to realise the retardatory force which that 
vacuum brake must be exerting,--it did not seem at all surprising that 
the train should have been brought to an almost instant stand-still. 

simultaneous all three of us were on our feet. i let down my window 
and atherton let down his,--he shouting out, 

'i should think that inspector's wire hasn't had it's proper effect, 
looks as if we're blocked--or else we've stop at luton. it can't be 
bedford.' 

it wasn't bedford--so much seem clear. though at first from my window 
i could make out nothing. i was feeling more than a trifle 
dazed,--there was a singing in my ears,--the sudden dark was 
impenetrable. then i became conscious that the guard was opening the 
door of his compartment. he stood on the step for a moment, seeming to 
hesitate. then, with a lamp in his hand, he descended on to the line. 

'what's the matter?' i asked. 

'don't know, sir. seem as if there was something on the road. what's 
up there?' 

this was to the man on the engine. the fireman replied: 

'someone in front there's wave a red light like mad,--lucky i caught 
sight of him, we should have been clean on top of him in another 
moment. looks as if there was something wrong. here he comes.' 

as my eyes grew more accustomed to the dark i became aware that 
someone was making what haste he could along the six-foot way, swinging 
a red light as he came. our guard advanced to meet him, shouting as he 
went: 

'what's the matter! who's that?' 

a voice replied, 

'my god! is that george hewett. i thought you were coming right on top 
of us!' 

our guard again. 

'what! jim branson! what the devil are you do here, what's wrong? i 
thought you were on the twelve out, we're chase you.' 

'are you? then you've caught us. thank god for it!--we're a wreck.' 

i had already open the carriage door. with that we all three 
clamber out on to the line. 




chapter xlvii 

the contents of the third-class carriage 


i move to the stranger who was holding the lamp. he was in official 
uniform. 

'are you the guard of the 12.0 out from st pancras?' 

'i am.' 

'where's your train? what's happened?' 

'as for where it is, there it is, right in front of you, what's left of 
it. as to what's happened, why, we're wrecked.' 

'what do you mean by you're wrecked?' 

'some heavy loaded truck broke loose from a goods in front and came 
running down the hill on top of us.' 

'how long ago was it?' 

'not ten minutes. i was just start off down the road to the signal 
box, it's a good two mile away, when i saw you coming. my god! i 
thought there was go to be another smash.' 

'much damage done?' 

'seems to me as if we're all smashed up. as far as i can make out 
they're matchbox up in front. i feel as if i was all broken up inside 
of me. i've been in the service go on for thirty years, and this is 
the first accident i've been in.' 

it was too dark to see the man's face, but judge from his tone he was 
either crying or very near to it. 

our guard turn and shout back to our engine, 

'you'd better go back to the box and let 'em know!' 

'all right!' came echo back. 

the special immediately commence retreating, whistle continual as 
it went. all the country side must have heard the engine shrieking, and 
all who did hear must have understood that on the line something was 
seriously wrong. 

the smashed train was all in darkness, the force of the collision had 
put out all the carriage lamps. here was a flicker candle, there the 
glimmer of a match, these were all the lights which shone upon the 
scene. people were pile up debris by the side of the line, for the 
purpose of making a fire,--more for illumination than for warmth. 

many of the passenger had succeed in free themselves, and were 
moving hither and thither about the line. but the majority appear to 
be still imprisoned. the carriage door were jammed. without the 
necessary tool it was impossible to open them. every step we took our 
ear were salute by piteous cries. men, women, children, appeal to 
us for help. 

'open the door, sir!' 'in the name of god, sir, open the door!' 

over and over again, in all sort of tones, with all degree of 
violence, the supplicate was repeated. 

the guard vain endeavour to appease the, in many cases, 
half-frenzied creatures. 

'all right, sir! if you'll only wait a minute or two, madam! we can't 
get the door open without tools, a special train's just start off to 
get them. if you'll only have patience there'll be plenty of help for 
everyone of you directly. you'll be quite safe in there, if you'll only 
keep still.' 

but that was just what they found it most difficult to do--keep still! 

in the front of the train all was chaos. the truck which had done the 
mischief--there were afterwards shown to be six of them, together with 
two guards' vans--appeared to have been laden with bags of portland 
cement. the bags had burst, and everything was cover with what seem 
gritty dust. the air was full of the stuff, it got into our eyes, half 
blind us. the engine of the express had turn a complete 
somersault. it vomit forth smoke, and steam, and flames,--every 
moment it seem as if the woodwork of the carriage immediately behind 
and beneath would catch fire. 

the front coach were, as the guard had put it, 'match-boxed.' they 
were nothing but a heap of debris,--telescoped into one another in a 
state of apparently inextricable confusion. it was broad daylight 
before access was gain to what had once been the interiors. the 
condition of the first third-class compartment reveal an 
extraordinary state of things. 

scattered all over it were pieces of what look like partially burnt 
rags, and fragment of silk and linen. i have those fragment now. 
expert have assured me that they are actually neither of silk nor 
linen! but of some material--animal rather than vegetable--with which 
they are wholly unacquainted. on the cushion and woodwork--especially 
on the woodwork of the floor--were huge blotches,--stains of some sort. 
when first notice they were damp, and gave out a most unpleasant 
smell. one of the pieces of woodwork is yet in my possession,--with the 
stain still on it. expert have pronounced upon it too,--with the 
result that opinion are divided. some maintain that the stain was 
produce by human blood, which had been subject to a great heat, and, 
so to speak, parboiled. other declare that it is the blood of some 
wild animal,--possibly of some creature of the cat species. yet other 
affirm that it is not blood at all, but merely paint. while a fourth 
describe it as--i quote the written opinion which lie in front of 
me--'caused apparently by a deposit of some sort of viscid matter, 
probably the excretion of some variety of lizard.' 

in a corner of the carriage was the body of what seem a young man 
costume like a tramp. it was marjorie lindon. 

so far as a most careful search revealed, that was all the compartment 
contained. 




chapter xlviii 

the conclusion of the matter 


it is several years since i bore my part in the event which i have 
rapid sketched,--or i should not have felt justified in give them 
publicity. exactly how many years, for reason which should be 
sufficient obvious, i must decline to say. 

marjorie lindon still lives. the spark of life which was left in her, 
when she was extricate from among the debris of the wreck express, 
was fan again into flame. her restoration was, however, not merely 
an affair of week or months, it was a matter of years. i believe that, 
even after her physical powers were completely restored--in itself a 
tedious task--she was for something like three years under medical 
supervise as a lunatic. but all that skill and money could do was 
done, and in course of time--the great healer--the result were 
entire satisfactory. 

her father is dead,--and has left her in possession of the family 
estates. she is married to the individual who, in these pages, has been 
known as paul lessingham. were his real name divulge she would be 
recognise as the popular and universally reverence wife of one of the 
greatest statesmen the age has seen. 

nothing has been said to her about the fateful day on which she 
was--consciously or unconsciously--paraded through london in the 
tattered masculine habiliment of a vagabond. she herself has never 
once allude to it. with the return of reason the affair seem to have 
pass from her memory as wholly as if it had never been, which, 
although she may not know it, is not the least cause she has for 
thankfulness. therefore what actually transpire will never, in all 
human probability, be certainly known and particularly what precisely 
occur in the railway carriage during that dreadful moment of sudden 
passing from life unto death. what became of the creature who all but 
did her to death; who he was--if it was a 'he,' which is extremely 
doubtful; whence he came; whither he went; what was the purport of his 
presence here,--to this hour these things are puzzles. 

paul lessingham has not since been trouble by his old tormentor. he 
has cease to be a haunt man. none the less he continue to have what 
seem to be a constitutional disrelish for the subject of beetles, nor 
can he himself be induce to speak of them. should they be mention in 
a general conversation, should he be unable to immediately bring about 
a change of theme, he will, if possible, get up and leave the room. 
more, on this point he and his wife are one. 

the fact may not be generally known, but it is so. also i have reason 
to believe that there still are moments in which he hark back, with 
something like physical shrinking, to that awful nightmare of the past, 
and in which he pray god, that as it is distant from him now so may it 
be kept far off from him for ever. 

before closing, one matter may be casual mentioned. the tale has 
never been told, but i have unimpeachable authority for its 
authenticity. 

during the recent expeditionary advance towards dongola, a body of 
native troops which was encamp at a remote spot in the desert was 
arouse one night by what seem to be the sound of a loud explosion. 
the next morning, at a distance of about a couple of mile from the 
camp, a huge hole was discover in the ground,--as if blast 
operations, on an enormous scale, had recently been carry on. in the 
hole itself, and round about it, were found fragment of what seem 
bodies; credible witness have assured me that they were body 
neither of men nor women, but of creature of some monstrous growth. i 
prefer to believe, since no scientific examination of the remains took 
place, that these witness ignorantly, though innocently, erred. 

one thing is sure. numerous pieces, both of stone and of metal, were 
seen, which went far to suggest that some curious subterranean building 
had been blown up by the force of the explosion. especially were there 
portion of mould metal which seem to belong to what must have been 
an immense bronze statue. there were picked up also, more than a dozen 
replica in bronze of the whilom sacred scarabaeus. 

that the den of demon describe by paul lessingham, had, that night, 
at last come to an end, and that these things which lay scattered, here 
and there, on that treeless plain, were the evidence of its final 
destruction, is not a hypothesis which i should care to advance with 
any degree of certainty. but, putt this and that together, the fact 
seem to point that way,--and it is a consummation devoutly to be 
desired. 

by-the-bye, sydney atherton has married miss dora grayling. her wealth 
has made him one of the richest men in england. she began, the story 
goes, by loving him immensely; i can answer for the fact that he has 
end by loving her as much. their devotion to each other contradict 
the pessimist nonsense which suppose that every marriage must be of 
necessity a failure. he continue his career of an inventor. his 
investigate into the subject of aerial flight, which have brought 
the flying machine within the range of practical politics, are on 
everybody's tongue. 

the best man at atherton's wedding was percy woodville, now the earl of 
barnes. within six month afterwards he married one of mrs atherton's 
bridesmaids. 

it was never certainly shown how robert holt came to his end. at the 
inquest the coroner's jury was content to return a verdict of 'died of 
exhaustion.' he lie bury in kensal green cemetery, under a handsome 
tombstone, the cost of which, had he had it in his pockets, might have 
indefinitely prolonged his days. 

it should be mention that that portion of this strange history which 
purport to be the surprising narration of robert holt was compile 
from the statement which holt made to atherton, and to miss lindon, as 
she then was, when, a mud-stained, shatter derelict he lay at the 
lady's father's house. 

miss linden's contribution towards the elucidate of the mystery was 
written with her own hand. after her physical strength had come back to 
her, and, while mentally, she still hover between the dark and 
the light, her one relaxation was writing. although she would never 
speak of what she had written, it was found that her theme was always 
the same. she confide to pen and paper what she would not speak of 
with her lips. she told, and re-told, and re-told again, the story of 
her love, and of her tribulation so far as it is contained in the 
present volume. her mss. invariable began and end at the same point. 
they have all of them been destroyed, with one exception. that 
exception is herein placed before the reader. 

on the subject of the mystery of the beetle i do not propose to 
pronounce a confident opinion. atherton and i have talk it over many 
and many a time, and at the end we have got no 'forrarder.' so far as i 
am personally concerned, experience has taught me that there are indeed 
more things in heaven and earth than are dream of in our philosophy, 
and i am quite prepared to believe that the so-called beetle, which 
other saw, but i never, was--or is, for it cannot be certainly shown 
that the thing is not still existing--a creature born neither of god 
nor man.
